DO ANY OF THE FOLLOWING SYMPTOMS,

,nd energy for business pursuits ? Are you subject

symptoms? If you are afflicted, why further neglect
your health or future happiness, when by a single con-

DR. FLETCHER’S
TREATISE ON NERVOUS DISEASES, THEIR CAUSE,
SYMPTOMS, TREATMENT, AND CURE.
THE directions contained in this little treatise will be
* found of immense benefit to the sufferer; and if these
directions are observed during the early stages of Nervous
or Sexual Debility, No Medicines will he required, as, by
observing the directions given, the patient can Cure Him-
self.
This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indis-
cretions in early life—overwork, irregularity in eating or
drinking, &c. It is the result of much study and long
and successful experience in the treatment of diseases,
and contains a fund of useful information to every suf-
ferer from the following complaints, viz. Nervous
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the
Back, Defective Memory, Loss of Will Power, Loss of
Energy, Spermatorrhoea, Bashfulness, Discontentedness,
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy,
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart, Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.
All Should Send for this Little Treatise,
If the complaint is of long standing and requires
medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CURE
ANY CASE OF NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILITY
with ONE COURSE OF MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send with medicines A WRITTEN
GUARANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Dr. Fletcher’s treatment is both simple and effective,
and within the reach of the poorest. It is unlike any
other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, and
invites patients to investigate his statements by verifying
the testimonials published in his treatise. The treatise
will be forwarded on receipt of two stamps to cover
postage.

(REGISTERED BY THE MEDICAL BOARD OF NEW SOUTH WALES),
Late House Surgeon to the Great Northern Hospital, Caledonian Road, London,
SPECIALIST in the Treatment of Diseases of the
Reproductive Organs and Nervous Complaints,
Du. PERRY was induced to publicly advertise himself as a
Specialist in this branch of the profession in consequence of the number
of persons suffering from diseases of this nature remaining mneured; and
this is not surprising, when it is remembered, in consequence of the
general dislike of the profession to undertake these cases, the public
health has suffered, because the majority of persons advertising to treat
these diseases, and calling themselves Doctors, Professors, etc.,
Are Neither the One nor the Other,
and exist chiefly because DULY QUALIFIED MEDICAL PRAC-
TITIONERS have not occupied the field.
Patients can, therefore, readily see the advantage of being treated
by a practitioner who has gone through a thorough Medical, Surgical and
Anatomical Training in the best hospitals ; and the Doctor argues that,
although it may be considered contrary to antiquated medical etiquette
to so advertise, yet he is promoting the usefulness and the dignity of
the profession more by searching out and relieving this class of disease,
than by leaving them severely alone to the care of men who, however good
their intentions, have neither the opportunities or advantages of a regular
medical training to become acquainted with the best methods of treating
these complex forms of disease.

READ THIS
Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralism. —Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, hut
oven the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
the important features of the district are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeats itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,
the Capital of West Australia.

SATURDAY. AUGUST 2, 18S0,
Liquor and Law.
Teetotallers have waged so able and per-
sistent a crusade against the State sanction
of the traffic in alcoholic drinks, that the
(piestion involved ranks to-day among those
which constitute the first concern of national
politics. In the old world and the new the
question has commanded and commands
urgent attention, while here in Australia it
is vital, vigorous, an;l exacting of serious
consideration. These facts are creditable to
the pugnacity, perseverance, and argumen-
tative capacity of water-propagandists gener-
ally. There is merit in the cause itself, it
is true—and so much merit that if every
water-drinker in civilisation had been stricken
dumb twenty years ago a host of minds would
be to-day seriously engaged on the pro-
blem. But, notwithstanding, without teetotal
enthusiasm, teetotal arguments, and the
contumacious perseverance of the disciplined
and undisciplined battalions of the water-
cult, the popular mind and the general poli-
tical mind would still look upon proposals to
wholly suppress intoxicants by legislation as
the fanciful tilts of vino-phobiac Quixotes
astride of granite-fountain Rosinantes.
# # #
In reality, aggressive teetotallers have had,
and have, on the body social and political,
an analogous influence and effect' to those
which spirituous liquor has on the individual
consumer. They have, acting in their sphere,
quickened the pulse of the community, and
refined its perception of the evils associated
with drink. For centuries communities took
no note of their drink-bills. They attached
no odium to the excessive drinker merely
because he drank to excess. Early civilisa-
tion is a record of victorious achievements
crowned with the goblet, and of national
disasters whose bitterness it assuaged. Civili-
sation slept and dreamed through the long
Middle Ages, and all the time with the odour
>f wine on its breath and its generous fire
in its veins ; and when it arose and shook
itself and prepared to answer to modern
lemands, it marched forth to its task with
the vine-branch in one hand and the charter
of human rights in the other. Religious
festivities summed themselves up in the
ivine-eup, and visions of Walhalla revealed
the immortal gods engaged in divine con-
viviality.
# - # #
The conscience of the world was not yet
alive to the evils which intoxicants carry in
their train, and must have carried since the
first man drank to excess. No pictures of
shattered health, of blighted reputations, of
blasted prospects, arose before the mind.
No haggard mothers or starved and stunted
children met the gaze. Around the sparkling
juice of the grape lay an atmosphere in
which only prismatic lines of joy and
good - fellowship were discernible. This
atmosphere aggressive teetotallers pierced,
dispersed. The coarse crayon of untutored
earnestness drew the drunkard as a mur-
derer of wife and children ; the pencil and
brush of draughtsman and painter limned
iiim as the maker of paupers and material of
maniacs. The specti’al horrors stalked
around the banqueting-room, out into the
streets, and crowded the market places.
Observation showed with what terrible fre-
quency these ghastly apparitions were
materialised, and as the teetotaller invoked
aid the communal mind, excited, inflamed,
fell, or came near falling, under his spell,
# * *
But just as unlimited subjection to
spirituous liquors precipitates the individual
into physical drunkenness, so unlimited sub-
jection to this enthusiasm or any enthusiasm
precipitates to mental drunkenness. The
laws which, under the influence of this new
propaganda, banished intoxicants from
several of the States of America bear all the
characteristics of the products of drunken
minds. They were vitiated by suddenness,
barbarised by injustice, and stultified by
inconsequentiality. A day was enough to
set them up ; a day was enough in which to
knock them down. At one fell swoop they
deprived a man of a means of living which,
until the teetotallers hung their pictures
out, they and he had regarded as honest.
They drove nails into the doors of public-
houses and rejoiced as victors ; but the secret
distiller and the chemist carried on the
business in private houses or they swept
one ward or town clear of the traffic only to
make the contiguous places cess-pools for
both. This brings us to our moral : Some
teetotaller and some publican is good ; too
much teetotaller or too much publican is
bad. Without the teetotaller the world
would, as we have said, be still dull to many
of the evils of intemperance ; with him as
the rider, drunken minds will assume the
cure of drunken bodies, and intelligent
reform be retarded.
# # *
Drunkenness is no more in wine than is
gluttony in meat. The bibulant is attracted
by the wine-cup, the glutton by the steaming
joint. The excesses are human acts and to be
corrected, not by breaking the wine-cup, not
by penalising cooked joints, but by operating
on the capacity for restraint which adult
human beings, not devoid of reason, must
be assumed to possess. The man physically
drunk smashes unoffending tables and chairs ;
the man mentally drunk demands power to
smash the unoffending wine-glass. Where,
once complied with, is this mental inebriety
to stop ? Shall the vegetarian strive for a
majority to apply legal prohibition to beef-
eating ; shall the beef-eater similarly pro-
hibit the vegetarian ? Shall the athlete call
up a majority to destroy the books of the
recluse, and the recluse to burn the imple-
ments of the athlete ? Shall the smoker slay
the non-smoker with a majority, or the latter
the smoker ? Men have damaged health by
too much meat, by too much vegetables, by
too great an addition of sport or study, and
by too much tobacco. Of course the evils in
these cases are neither so widespread nor
intense, save in the case of over-eating when
viewed by the practised medical eye, but
evils ilv arise under them, and once the prin-
ciple of prohibiting by law the exercise of an
appetite which is moderate because other
appetites in the same line are immoderate, is
admitted, the duty of morally disciplining
the individual ceases in all directions, and
the vole of a Parliamentary or other majority
must be brought up and put in its place.
# * *
The evils arising from sexual passion are
more numerous and deadly than all other
evils taken together. Love, inordinate, ill-
regulated, has filled more charnel - houses
than all the red and white wine that ever
came from the winepress. Under its gaze
youth has staggered, dazed to a premature
grave, and age has gone blindly to dishonour.
Because in many cases Love is a road to ruin,
because in many cases marriage is a road to
fatal strife, who will advocate that love is to
be barred off and marriage made impossible?
But it is on such a principle that Local
Option and Prohibition rest. The right to
love, the right to marry, the right to eat, the
right to drink—-all are inalienable, and only
when trespassing upon one another or upon
the community they lose their character
and become wrongs can the function of
Government be properly or profitably brought
to bear on them.
* # #
The Drink traffic wants reform, but the
reform to be sound, to be lasting, must rest
on the maintenance, not on the deprivation,
of natural rights. The traffic is dangerous
and acts overtly and covertly on the welfare
of society. Society invokes, therefore, a
power to eliminate from the traffic
whatever leads in a direction contrary
to the duties of good citizenship. All
this it can do, and the traffic will still
survive. It can, for instance, increase the
license-fees and insist on spacious liars. The
first will make the holder of the license
regard forfeiture with terror, the second will
give a semi-public character to the perform-
ance of drinking, and many of the insidious
practices alleged to be at present current
will become thereby impossible. These two
reforms will, of themselves, bo sufficient to
root out the low dons where painted harri-
dans and soaked dupes play with one another’s
lives. And with this gained public decency
and national honour will be safer than after
a universal mutilation sanctioned by a
majority, ready, for a mere fad, to cut the
throat of personal liberty.
An Eight-Hours Bill.
Sir Samuel Griffith, in his other capacity as the
friend of toil, has introduced in the Queensland.
Assembly a Bill which ought to,, make his name
famous. The ostensible objects of this literary
effort are twofold ; in the first place it lays down
the axiom that eight hours ought to constitute a
day’s labour, and in the second it states that if it
does not do so the Legislature feels sorry for the
circumstance, but fails to see how it can help it.
The measure is not an enactment in the ordinary
sense of the term, for it doesn’t enact anything ;
and even if it is passed it cannot reasonably be
called a law, for it doesn’t require anybody to do
anything or to leave anything undone. Probably
it will be alluded to as an Act of Parliament; but
as it makes no pretence of acting in any way, the
name is a misnomer and a delusion. The Eight-
Hours’ Bill, in sirort, simply states that something
ought to be done, and makes no attempt to do it.
Sir Samuel himself describes it as a “ declaratory
measure,” and the description is about as accu-
rate as any mere earthly thing well can be;
for it declares in the first place that the em-
ployer ought to adopt a certain course, and in
■ the second that he can adopt it or not according
to how he feels on the subject. In this respect,
therefore, it declares twice as much as any
ordinary measure, and it is difficult to imagine
how any mortal ingenuity could well make it
declare more. It is crowded as full of declaration
as it can hold, and the same great natural law
which prevents a whale from getting into a jug or
an alligator from pushing itself through under a
keyhole, makes it impossible to insert any more—
there isn’t room for it. Still in one important
point it is all that its author’s fancy has painted
it. It is a fair, judicial and unbiassed measure
which does no harm to anybody. It legislates on
both sides of the question with equal impar-
tiality, and then abolishes itself, and stands out,
and leaves both parties to scramble for anything
they can get. It doesn’t interfere with the
liberty of the subject in any way. It simply
asserts its own existence, and tells everybody to
take no notice of it, and do as they darned well
please, and whichever faction comes uppermost
the Bill will fit the case.
# * #
It is a painful evidence of the innate cussedness
of Labour that tliis comprehensive measure has
been received with universal dissatisfaction. The
Bill is an elaborate statement of Sir Samuel’s
democratic sentiments, and it is with a look of
pained surprise that that eminent politician learns
that the country doesn’t care one solitary straw
for his sentiments so long as they do no good to
anyone but the owner. If Sir Samuel is of opinion
that the nation ought to have clothes, and yet the
nation hasn’t got them, the general idea is that
humanity will be just as naked as it was before,
and a Bill which practically states that the toiling
masses will be graciously permitted to do the best
they can, and that the State won’t hinder them if
it isn’t able to, is met by the uncourteous rejoinder
that the masses could do exactly the same thing
without any Bill at all. It is generously provided
that eight hours will be the limit of a day’s labour,
except when the employer arranges otherwise,
and unfortunately the employer invariably
arranges otherwise whenever he is in a position to
do so, and will keep up this habit till the end
of time. And yet it is on the strength of such
measures as this that Queensland’s exploded boss-
democrat has built his hollow reputation as a
Tribune of the People, and has successfully per-
suaded a large section of the nation that the
gilded dog-collar of St, Michael and St. George
is the emblem of liberty and national progress.
He has yearned all through his political career
over the troubles and sufferings of the poor, and
has always been on the verge of doing something
which was never done, and initiating some shape-
less, intangible reform which was to start in an
unspecified manner, and result in something that
was never very clearly defined. And the final
result of all these years of empty theory is, that
the people are to take anything they can get, and
get it anyhow they can, and if they get nothing
at all, the Tribune of the People will get there
just the same.
* # #
Still the democratic party in Queensland cannot
fairly denounce Sir Samuel Griffith as a rene-
gade from his ancient faith, for so far as the party
was concerned he never owned a faith, except in
theory. He has not gone back on his record for
he had no record at all. He is a species of hybrid
republican who would gladly fill the post of
monarch in a free commonwealth, and he longs to
promote an era of universal equality, so long as
there is a hereditary aristocracy in it, and a House
of Peers, and a privileged guild of lawyers,
and provided that all the ancient land-
marks are left standing, and no venerable prece-
dent is interfered with. Neither princes nor
K.C.M.G.s of princely manufacture are of any use
to the cause of Labour. Even the sixpence-worth
of base metal and old junk which forms the in-
signia of the cheapest order of knighthood on
earth is too much to sacrifice for any vague yearn-
ings after social regeneration. Probably the two
loftiest and most conservative beings in this world
are-the colonial knight, in all the glory of a decora-
tion which is inferior to a busted jam-tin both in
point of ornament and utility, and the plush-clad
flunkey who shines the plate of a British lord, and
to the knight and the flunkey alike the idea that
mankind was born free and equal is abhorrent.
The one feels that his decoration is at stake ; the
other dreads an upheaval which may rend from
him his livery and his unnaturally expansive calves
stuffed with sawdust. And as a natural result
the cause which pins its faith to either of these
two elevated individuals is apt, sooner or later, to
find itself left.
* * #
Probably the very meanness of the mongrel
patent of Australian nobility adds to the rampant
conservatism of the wearer. The dignity of a
corporal is generally a much more overwhelming
thing than that of a general, and the sergeant, as
a rule, throws his chest out at least two feet
further than the field-marshal, and looks by far
the more important animal of the two. No species
of nobility is too cheap or too mean to place a
gulf between its owner and the untitled multitude,
but the so-called democrat who goes back on his
creed for the sake of the lowest grade in the Order
of the Gilded Id'ot, is often further removed from
all sympathy with the cause of democracy than
the hereditary peer whose race has been of no use
to anybody since the Conquest. Even the lofty
exclusiveness of the House of Guise was a small
affair compared to that of the insurrectionary
nigger whom the Emperor Dkssalines, of Hayti,
glorified under the title of the Duke of Marma-
lade ; and this abject reverence for small dis-
tinctions links the K.C.M.G. and the head waiter
together, and binds them both to the principal
counter-jumper in the fashionable drapery ware-
house, and establishes an indissoluble bond of
brotherhood between the old family flunkey
and the colonial privy councillor twin
emblems of the smallest and most insign ifi.
cant of human dignities. The far-reachin
selfishness and grovel of humanity makes the
cause of Democracy principally the cause of those
who have nothing to lose, and whether the bogus
aristocrat stakes'the inexpensive degradation of
St. Michael and St. George, or hazards only a
suit of plush, he is prepared in either ease to see
the world in ruins before he will let go. Even the
possession of a gin-shop with a coal-shed behind is
sufficient in most cases to make the owner a ram-
pant defender of the rights of Proputty in all its
aspects, and many a bitter Conservative has no
better grounds for the faith that is in him than a
small annuity and the fact that a remote relative
of his family married a lord who kicked her after-
wards. And, therefore, if the Labour party of
Queensland finds 1 hat its ex-idol is only a
lawyer and a K.C.M.G. after all, it may
console itself with the reflection that it would
have been contrary to (iOOO years of human ex-
perience if he had proved to be anything else.
The old Saying, “ Put not your trust in Princes,”
was based on a knowledge of the fact that a prince
is generally a mean animal and of no account;
and a K.C.M.G., at the best, is only a thing that
a prince has manufactured, and usually a thing
he has dashed off in a hurry as being a matter of
no importance. And it is absurd to expect the
object created to be grander than the creator, even
if the creator hadn’t made it anyhow and then
shoved it out of the way because he was anxious
to go to the theatre, or to have an epileptic fit, or
to pursue some other of the favourite avocations
of royalty.
Two Alligators or One Alligator?
Once more an attempt is being made in New South
Wales to pass an Act amalgamating the two
branches of the legal profession. This time the
Bill has originated in the- Upper House, and for
once Salomons, Q.C., and ex-Judge Manning,
who usually disagree about everything on prin-
ciple, have joined forces to overthrow this demo-
cratic measure. There has been so much “law”
lately that everybody knows the difference between
the two branches of the legal profession. In con-
ducting a case it is the duty of the attorney to see
the witnesses, collect and sift their evidence, take
down their statements and make out a written
precis of the case, called a brief, which he hands to
the barrister ; the latter conducts the case in court,
examines and cross-examines witnesses, addresses
the jury, and argues any points of law that may
arise. The client has to pay both these parties,
# # *
The original practice in New South Wales was
to make no arbitrary division between legal prac-
titioners. If a client chose to do so, he could
have one man conduct his case right through. The
same man that saw the witnesses, and took their
statements, and knew the whole details of the
case, and with whom the client had personally
talked the matter oyer, could, if the client so
chose, conduct it in court. But that sort of thing
did not suit the Judges of later times. They
wanted to follow England, to create a “ Bar ” with
all its privileges, and its exclusiveness; so the
Judges, not the people nor the Legislature, mind
you, but the Judges, under some real or fancied
power given by the Act enabling them to make
rules for the conduct of business in court, made a
rule that from and after a certain time all cases,
big or little, must be conducted by a man with a
horsehair wig, instructed by another without any
wig, and that the public must, whether they like
it or not, employ two men to do the work of one.
They altered the old practice and introduced a
novelty for which the public had to pay the piper.
* * «
So that the present Bill is really an attempt to
return to the old order of things, and not an
innovation in any way. The Bill simply
asks that the public shall be allowed to
employ one man right through a cake, if
they, the public, choose to do so. And against
this very reasonable request, what arguments do
the opponents of the. Bill put forward ? Mainly
this —that the functions of barrister and attorney
are so different that no one man can properly at-
tend to both duties in working up a case. But
this argument only applies to large cases. Where
there are many witnesses to see, many documents
to peruse, and a lot of points of law to work up, it
is no doubt to the client’s own interest to employ
two men to manage the case. But surely this
should be left to the client's own discretion, and
the employment of two men where he only wishes
to employ one should not be forced upon him by
the court. It will be to his own loss if the
attorney mismanages the case. We repeat that
it is only in large cases that any necessity for the
employment of two men exists. There are hun-
dreds of mere debt actions tried every year which
any intelligent layman could conduct after a little
experience, but the barrister must have his fee in
these cases just as in the large ones. In cases
where a defence is entered solely to gain time, the
plaintiff has to add a barrister’s fee to the debt
which he is striving to collect, and which he will
in all probability never recover. The costs of the
simpler actions are thereby enormously increased,
and law and justice are made expensive luxuries to
the public.
# * *
As to the argument that if attorneys are allowed
to appear in Court they will mismanage cases and
waste the time of the Judge, there is nothing in
this contention. If any person, attorney or bar-
rister, mismanages cases he will soon have none
put in his hands, so that this evil will work its
own cure. As to delay, it is notorious that the
prevention of delay lies almost wholly in the
hands of the presiding Judge. That great Judge,
Sir James Martin, used to keep the Bar up to
their work. There was no waste of time in c ases
tried before him. An incompetent barrister would
put a laboured and intricate question to a witness
and the old man would break in with his sharp,
quick utterance, “No good asking him that, he
doesn't understand you.” And then he would put
to the witne : s the pith of the question himself in
about five words. Or if a barrister repeated him-
self— “ You-said-that-before-no-good-saying-it-
again.” If an irrelevant question was asked he
would blurt out, “Nothing to do with the case,
can’t ask him that.” Cases tried before him were
never delayed ; but, now-a-days, having some
miserably weak men on the Bench, the leading
barristers simply run riot over them, waste time
(whereby they earn refreshers), insult the Judge,
quarrel fiercely with each other, and spin a case
out over days and days. The remedy for delay is
in the Judge’s hands, and several cases lately in
Sydney have been notoriously spun out through
the Judge not keeping control of the counsel.
Without being on the spot we can on’y hazard a
guess, but we think that if any prom]it and de-
cisive official were hearing the Premier Permanent
Building Society prosecution it would have been
over long ago.
* * *
The two main arguments against amalgamation
are thus disposed of. There are a host of detail
arguments which are of amazing importance in
the eyes of the profession—for instance, whether
advocacy fees should be taxable, whether theadvo-


cate should be responsible to the client if he
neglected the case, and so forth. There is no
difficulty whatever in dealing with these details.
Make your lawyer one man, responsible for every-
thing he does or omits to do; make all his charges
taxable; and if you think the necessities of the
case demand it, and you have money enough,
employ two men—one to work up the case and one
to conduct it. Even if the amalgamation had no
effect in clxeapening big cases, it would certainly
cheapen small ones ; and even in big cases a man
would very likely prefer to have his case conducted
by the maix who had an intimate knowledge of it
from the start.
About two months ago a raid was made on the
trains entering Melbourne from the suburbs, and
in the course of three hours 150 individuals were
found travelling without tickets, and thereby per-
petrating a deliberate swindle on the country at
large. There were three clergymen among the
delinquents, a lawyer or two, and about a dozen
merchants and other business men, to say nothing
of sundry well-known ladies who showed signs of
tears and hysterics. Nothing followed. Nobody
was prosecuted. The credit of “sassiety” was at
stake and “sassiety”—which consists, when you
look at it closely, of the red-faced woman with
the dog, and the retired publican’s “missus,”
and the pawnbroker who flourished by ad-
vancing money on the shirt and the bed-
stead and became a “shindykit,” and the
person who floated the gold-mine which had no
gold in it, and the solicitor who hasn’t been
found out —no doubt managed to choke the matter
off. Consequently Melbourne was spared the
spectacle of a number of ladies well known at
Government House fainting about in a police-
court on a charge of attempting to swindle the
State out of three coppers, and the merchant
princes of Victoria did not roll up to explain why
they were ready to sink to wholesale lying and
prevarication on the off-chance of saving a shill-
ing. But an old man who embezzled a jug valued
at about ninepence promptly got three months.
This is a great country.
• ♦
South Australia is considering the advisability
of making parsons eligible to serve as members of
Parliament. For our own part we see no reason
why they should not be eligible, except that we
want to know how the flock will get on while the
shepherd is away firing his old disused sermons at
his bleeding country, and introducing the left-off
hymn into his motion for adjournment ? Who is
to visit the widow and the fatherless when the
spiritual consoler is busy bringing in a Bill to
wallop the prisoner who won’t pray regularly in
gaol, or drafting a resolution to suppress anybody
who is found anywhere but at choir-practice after
10 p.m? Also, if the reverend legislator lives in
the country, and can’t get back in time for Sun-
day, who is to pound the reading-desk into unholy
smash, and bang the pulpit into shreds and
flinders while he tells how Balaam owned a mule
in the far East ? Who is to come down on the
sinner like countless loads of bricks if the apostle,
who,se voice ought to be lifted with a steam crane
owing to its unnatural dimensions, is away collect-
ing carnal honorarium and other unregenerate
perquisites of the flesh, and is arguing with the
ungodly about the remunerations of this transient
earth, and scrambling among the children of dark-
ness for railways and other fleeting things, all of
which is vanity ?

been fined £lOO, and sentenced to six months’ im-
prisonment and five years’ police surveillance, fox-
charging 180 per shent. on his little bill. The idea
isn’t a bad one, so far as it goes; but a more
sensible plan vvould have been to make him lend
his entire capital out oxx personal security at
per shent and to allow the borrower to assault
him if he refused to renew. Few things would
have a more saddening effect on the average
money-lender than a loan at 2i per cent., repay-
able at an indefinite date, the lender to stand the
price of the stamps. All of which reminds us of
the following, clipped from the report of an insol-
vency examination at Sydney the other day
Re Frances Amelia M‘Nab (a widow). The official
assignee called Abraham Abrahams, who was examined
by Mr. E. A. Smith. He was a partner of Morris Neu-
stadfc, named in the hill of sale dated August 21, 1889.
Of the first advance of £lOO only £7O was paid to the
bankrupt, £3O being kept back for interest. On August
27 £OO was advanced to pay off Isaac’s bill of sale, and
£23 was kept back for intei-est. He did not think it
necessary to state the amounts kept back for interest in
making a copy of the account to be produced in court.
On December 23 £BO was advanced, £4O of which was
kept back for interest. Bankrupt thus borrowed £BO, and
only got £4O of it. The item on December 20, £1 145.,
was a half-year’s premium for insuring Mrs, M'Nab’s life ;
ho got a policy for £2OO. On January 15 she made a pay-
ment of £l5, and he received another of £l5 on February
7 by letter from Murray Brothers, who were trustees for
Mrs. M‘Nab. The first he heard of the assignment was a
note from them, which letter he had destroyed. He saw
Mrs. M‘Nab, and asked her if she had signed any docu-
ment, and she said, “ None whatever.” His position was
a good one ; he could pay 40s. in the £.

One of the ablest contributions to the, Ibsen con-
troversy lately raging in Sydney is from the pen
of Rose de Boheme in the columns of the Sydney
Morn ino Herald, Dismissing the restraints
imposed by conventionality on thought—customs
out of which society will grow as surely as it has
out of narrower ones—the lady deals witlx the
question from the standpoint furnished by the
words of old Polonlus : “To thine ownself be
true,” and then, coinciding with his conclusive de-
duction, “and it must follow, as the night the day,
thou canst not then be false to any man, ” proceeds
to show that the desertion of husband and children
by wife and mother for spiritual reasons may be a
wholly unselfish, nay, a consciously self-sacrificial
act, and calculated in the ultimate to be highly
beneficial to all concerned and particularly to the
children. As she puts it;—
Ay, “ the children ” have proved the safety of many a
household, but they have, alas ! ofc proved its bane. We
nave all known parents kept together by children who
have learned to curse them ; we have known men and
women who, sacrificing their mutual repulsion for the
sake of the children, have drifted into a life of shame ; we
have known pure and true-hearted fathers and mothers,
manacled together for life, and tearing at their fetters till
the children learned to think of them with contempt ;
and, seeing all this, we may well pause ami ponder the
query; “ Was llo t Nora Helmbr justified by her
children?”
Prior to this the writer, gratefully acknowledging
the sympathetic discrimination ” with which the
question had been handled by men, denies them
any apprehension of the theory that Nora’s deser-
tion of her little ones may have been undertaken
with a view to their moral benefit as well as her
own. The inference to be drawn is plain : the sex,
none of whose members could correctly diagnose
the subtle promptings of a mother’s disinterested
love, is clearly incapable of such self-abnegating
acts. Unfortunately for feminine self-glorification,
this view of Nora’s conduct has already been
taken by some of her enthusiastic male sup-
porters, not only in print (vide D.T), but in
Private controversy. Within the sacred precincts
of this office the question was thoroughly threshed
out from this very standpoint by some ardent
Ibsenites many months ago. This little piece of,
shall we say sexegotism, is perhaps but a trifling
return for many slights levelled from the male
side, but nevertheless, like many other little
straws agitated by the breath of the women who
step out from among the “dumb, driven cattle”
of their sex, apparently signifies that, equality
assured, the banner bearing the motto “ Women’s
Rights ” will only be unfurled in order to unfold
one emblazoned with a new watchword
“Women’s Supremacy.’' That won’t happen in
sthe course of next week, however.

In the Universal Review, Tolstoi replies at
length to the critics of the “Kreutzer Sonata.”
Here are his propositions as summarised by the
Pall Mall. Gazette
1. That society tolerates, to the point of encouragement,
irregularity before marriage on the side of the man.
2. And, as a natural sequel, condones infidelity after
marriage.
3. Owing to the grossness of view on which these
practices are based, the birth of children has lost its
pristine significance.
4. Both the birth and education of children are subor-
dinated to the pleasure and convenience of the parents.
5. The fault lies with the idealisation of the material
side of love, which misleads and degrades the whole
human nature upon both sides. “ Love ” (that is, passion)
“and all the slates which accompany it and follow it,
however we may try in prose and verse to prove the con-
trary, never do, and never can, facilitate the attainment of
any aim worthy of men, but always make it more
difficult.”
There is much truth in all this, in fact nothing
that is not, speaking relatively, true, but in
Tolstoi’s deductions that monasticism is the only
pure bodily state, and that science and religion
are botli against the perpetuation of the race,
none but the mystic pessimists will be found to
agree with him.

The big daily newspapers were published again in
Melbourne on cablegram Sabbath —and thus
was committed a flagrant breach of the
law recently devised and instituted to crush
Bailey’s proposed Sunday Times ; but as
tlie offenders are rich and “ respectable, ” not to
say powerful, of course nobody will be prosecuted
by a Government which simply dare not declare
war against the Age and Argus, and which deli-
berately passed a Bill to crush a man who was
about to interfere with the dailies’ monopoly by
publishing a Sunday paper. There is thus in SNc.-
toria one law for Boss Stme and Wilson and Mac-
kinnon, and quite another law for the —er —un-
authorised organs of opinion. Such a state of
affairs is filthily indecent in view of the fact that if
the Age and Argus chose to suddenly round upon
Premier Gillies and his shady political gang the
latter wonld give up the ghost within a fortnight.
All these things help on the Liberation movement.
The minority, which some people fancy are the
majority, make a rush for the Sunday papers out
of mere spite, and a Bulletin man heard one little
newsboy say lie had made 18 shillings during the
day. Bailey will be round again when the
Library is open.
Words of wisdom from the Belfast (Ireland)
Morning News :
We are not over sanguine as to the possibility of
establishing a community of interests , between an old
country which has a weakness for coercive legislation and
young nationalities that are too honest and too brave to
have recourse to handcuffs and muzzles. Between Great
Britain’s old blue-blooded classes and the newer world’s
industrial masses the difficulties of partnership must be
almost insurmountable. Wealth only exists when the
working world creates it, and partnership between the
idle classes and the industrial masses must resolve itself
into the robbery or the starvation of one or other of the
two parties concerned. Hitherto we have had the rob-
bery and semi-starvation of labour, and should justice be
enforced in the future we shall see the reverse of the old
position. George Washington and his friends must have
understood this questionable community of interests and
federation idea pretty fairly when they elected for a free
hand at home and a strong arm to uphold it.

When Adam Linsday Gordon wrote—
No game was ever yet worth a rap
For a rational man to play,
Into which no accident, no mishap,
Could possibly find its way,
that gifted poet and steeplechase-rider didn’t
imagine that he was preparing a stock quotation
for the “sporting” drivellers of future genera-
tions. At this season of the year, when the in-
trepid “knight of the pigskin” (another ghastly
and indestructible gag) is breaking his neck more
than usual, Gordon’s jingle haunts us like our
shadow. The racing prophet works it into his
ruinous column of productions; the Special
Commissioner, or somebody, uses it to re-
lieve the dull monotony of his chestnut
“comments”; even the tout evinces a maudlin
desire to sling that bit of poetry among his
graphic, but utterly erroneous, descriptions of
morning gallops. In every obituary notice of a
steeplechase victim, human or otherwise, the sup-
position that “No game was ever yet,” &e., is
repeated. It has become a public nuisance and
an everlasting curse. It is the chief corner-stone
of a degraded sporting literature, and the constant
associate of disgusting grammar. Moreover, the
verse in question cannot claim even the prosaic
merit of truth. There is no danger to life or limb
in a regatta or a Sheffield handicap. Skittles is a
distinctly harmless sport, nor has any man ever
smashed his collar-bone at billiards, a game which,
when played under wholesome conditions, can do
more for brain and body than any other under the
sun. We respectfully move that Gordon’s too,
too familiar rhyme be warned off sporting papers
for 12 months.

Lawyer Tozer (Q.) should speak for himself, and
not set himself up as the political godfather of all
the Australian N atives, as he did in the recent
Parliamentary Federation debate. “Any desire
to separate from the Crown of Great Britain,”
said Tozer, “ is furthest from our thoughts. . .
We (the A. N.) never wish to be Separated from
the Crown of England, which was the origin of
these colonies, until in the process of time, by the
development of these colonies, the Crown of Eng-
land will see that it is to its advantage that Aus-
tralia should become a nation.” Australians, on
the contrary, desire to become a separate nation
as soon as ever they find it to their advantage to
realise and proclaim their nationhood. The crimes
of the Crown produced such a superabundance of
criminals that there was scarcely either food or
foothold for them in the old country, and then
“the Crown” transported those criminals to
Botany Bay. Australian natives are not op-
pressed with gratitude to the Crown for its having
been, in this sense, “ the origin of these colonies.”
Roses have grown on England’s dungheap just as,
by the alchemy of nature, sweet violeto might
spring from the dead leper’s breast.
The Melbourne Telegraph thus sums up Bierre
Douar, who lately suicided in Pentridge owing to
too much chains and “solitary ” ;—“Thedeceased
convict was believed to have been sent from
France to New Caledonia in 1880. What his
offence was the authorities here do not know. It
is sure to have been something desperate if not
oomplicatedly horrible. The extreme atrocity of
French cut-throats is proverbial.” Just so. The
organ of piety and Christian charity doesn’t know
what the offence was, and only “believes” that
Douar was in New Caledonia at all, but it is
quite sure that he was a cut-throat, and is
positive that he did something “ complicatedly
horrible.” And yet if the editor of the Tele-
graij h had lived in England some 80 short years
ago he might have been sent out in chains to
Botany Bay because lie wanted local self-govern-
or some other little trifle, and then another
man would be editing the Telegraph to-day, and
he would probably feel sure that Editor Fitchett
had strychnined his mother or hammered a long
nail into his wife’s head, because he had no evi-
dence to prove that the person who came out with
his head shaved in that bygone era had really done
anything at all. If a thing which possibly never
happened is a certain proof that the man whom it
probably never happened to, is a ruffian of the
deepest dye, then both Christianity and logic are
in a bad way.
The N.S.W. Typographical Union, after devoting
a night to the discussion of the subject, has with
much enthusiasm rejected a resolution in favour
of the, admission of female compositors to its
ranks. Mr. Fitzgerald, the mover, argued that
unless the women were permitted to work in har-
mony with the men, they would be driven to work
in non-union offices at lower wages, to the detri-
ment of their brother-compositors, and added that
a woman had every right to compete fairly with
a man in order to gain an honest living. Another
speaker pointed out that women took part in the
international typographical congresses in America,
and that they could not be kept out of the trade,
in which, he added, they did not stay long. Mr.
Watson said that female labour meant cheap
labour, that the printing-trade was too unhealthy
for women, for whom there were other avenues
of employment such as domestic service—a rather
cheeky remark, be it observed, considering that a
woman-compositor must have education whereas a
domestic servant who does not know “ b ” from a
bull’s foot commands as good pay as a servant who
can criticise the Young Ladies’ Journal. As to
the chivalric statement on the part of the male
compositors that the printing-trade is too un-
healthy for women—an objection, by the way,
which only applies in any special degree to
night-work in badly-arranged offices—we are
afraid the Union doth protest too much.
The final conclusion arrived at by the meeting—■
“That the employment of female labour in the
printing trade is opposed to the best interests of
this society”—contains a strong smack of con-
fessed selfishness. The Bulletin, as a journal
which has always supported trades-unionism, and
means to do so, while it is, as now, an absolute
necessity, takes the liberty of pointing out
that the Union’s course of action is fraught
with much danger. Suppose that some printing-
office began to employ qualified female composi-
tors, paying them on results at the same rates as
men, and the_ Union delared war against that
office, the Union would be beaten hands down.
For the labour federation would be divided on the
question, and-the women could make an irresist-
ible appeal for support to the general public. As
matters stand now, the refusal of the Union to
admit women only offers a premium to “rat”
employers to employ women and lower the stan-
dard wage.
Odds and Ends.
How is it that the Archbishop of Canter-
bury still exercises spiritual jurisdiction in
Australia? When a bishop has been duly elected
by the Diocesan Synod in Sydney, why can he not
be consecrated by one of the resident Australian
bishops as soon as he arrives, and enter on the
duties of his office immediately afterwards ? That
simple course, it seems—simply because it would
be too simple—is not possible. The bishop can
only be consecrated by the Archbishop of Canter-
bury, and that individual cannot proceed with the
business until he has obtained a license from the
Queen. But what lias the Queen, or the Arch-
bishop, got to do with ecclesiastical affairs? When
State Aid to Religion was abolished here years ago,
the old connection between the State and the Church
was abolished with it, and the two things fell
completely asunder. The Anglican Church is as
independent of the local and Imperial Govern-
ments as any of the dissenting churches ; it might
elect any of its own churchmen a bishop whenever
it pleases ; and if it can elect, why can it not con-
secrate ? The present practice is just as absurd as
if the British War Office were to obtain the
privilege of nominating the Major-General of our
Volunteers.
# * *
It is a curious fact that the eternal mar-
riage question should have become a popular topic
of discussion, not only in Australia, but in Eng-
land and Europe generally, during the present
year. A tidal-wave of conjugal excitement seems
to have swept over the minds of men and women
in many different parts of the world at the same
time. The newspapers and magasfines are loaded
with articles and correspondence on different
phases of the subject; every clause in a new
divorce bill is argued from every possible point of
view—religious and political, sensible and non-
sensical-while a new play, in which the whole
interest of the plot centres iff a novel development
of the relation between husband and wife, attracts
as many disputants as the question of Hamlet’s
madness. Ibsen’s play has added a new character
to the drama, in the shape of “ the ideal husband,”
the moral of it being that a wife is justified in
deserting her mate (and three children) as soon as
she finds that he is deficient in moral tone,
(esthetic susceptibility, or any other gaudy virtue
with which her youthful imagination may have
clothed his spiritual nakedness. This idea seems
to have captivated nearly all the married women
who have seen the play, probably because it was
a new revelation to nine-tenths of them that a
husband could have any spirituality about him.
Imagine the effect of it on a tradesman’s wife, who
lias been sanding the sugar, or selling short weight,
for years in order to put money in her husband’s
pocket unknown. The “guilty creature, sitting
at the play,” would begin to,speculate in her own
mind as to how she would act in the event of her
man turning round and abusing her the moment
she was found out.
* * *
Count Tolstoi, the Russian novelist, has
also worked out the ideal marriage question in his
latest novel, “The Kreutzer Sonata,” with such
remarkable effect that an American lady, who
was asked to translate the book into English, de-
clined the honour, on the ground that she had
never read anything like it in her life, and hoped
she never would again. Tolstoi undertakes to settle
the whole problem of several relations on a puiely
philosophic basis ; and the ground he goes upon is
that all romantic love, as he calls it—that is, love
in which the physical feeling is predominant—is a
fatal mistake, certain to involve both parties in
misery; the only love that can be warranted to
last being that which is “born of identity of senti-
ment, similarity of ideal, the friendship of the
sort.” This theory is calculated to create uneasy
sensations in the minds of all , young ladies and
gentlemen who believe in love at first sight as the
only correct card for the maiden stakes. What
will they say to this?-'“Our system that is,
our fashionable system—“is the worst possible,
and the whole of our wedding ceremonial—the
honeymoon, the feasting, and the incitement to
carnality, are directly calculated to result in the
depravation of matrimony. Not in one case out
of a hundred does romantic love result in life-long
happy union. It is like opium or hasheesh—the
sensation is overpowering and delightful; but it
passes. ” So the Count proposes to eliminate all
admiration of face and figure from future flirta-
tions, and to confine the delicious sentiment to an
interchange of ideas on the latest fashions or the
last new novel; and provided that opinions on
those subjects are found to coincide, an unroman-
tic marriage may be safely contracted.
* # #
One of the most sensible things ever said
about marriage will be found in “Lord Mel-
bourne’s Papers,” recently published. His lord-
ship’s perception was acute in all things, but on
this subject it was particularly sharpened by his
own experience. “ Before marriage the shape, the
figure, the complexion, carry all before them;
after marriage, the mind and character unex-
claim their share, and that the largest,
of importance. ” If it were possible to reverse this
arrangement, the Divorce-courts might be shut
up; but iiow is it to be done ? It will never
be done until wo adopt Dr. Johnsbn’s idea when
he,said that if any young people who wished to
marry were to go before a judge and stand a cross-
examination as to their suitability to each other,
he would be a much better judge of the subject
than they could be.
* * *
Sir George Bowen tells some good stories
in the two volumes of memoirs which he has
recently given to the world. About the best is
one of Admiral Gore Jones, whom he met at
Mauritius —the only admiral he ever knew who
could quote “Homer” and “Virgil.” “He told
me that he was the first-lieutenant of a ship which,
shortly before the Crimean War, took the kite
Lord Carlisle from Constantinople to Rhodes, and
that he happened to be on watch early one morn-
ing just as he was passing Tenedos. Lord Carlisle
came on deck and asked : ‘ What island is that ?’
when Gore Jones answered : ‘ Tenedos, my Lord,
the island of Apollo; you recollect Homer says
of that god that he was ‘ ruler of Tenedos ’
(quoting the original Greek). The admiral says
that Lord Carlisle staggered back and almost fell
down the companion-ladder, saying, in a low, awe-
struck voice : ‘Sir, you are the first naval officer I
ever heard quoting Greek ! 1 did not know such
a thing was possible !’ ”
But there is one well-authenticated story
which Bowen does not tell his readers. When the
Civil Service of Victoria was being decimated by
Graham Berry under his authority, the socially
astute and courtly gentleman with the wicked eyes,
who then held the office of Chief Commissioner of
Police, received an early intimation from a Mel-
bourne Club friend- that his name was on the list
of the doomed. Early next morning he presented
himself at Government House and, having been
received by Sir George with the usual blarney,
proceeded to state his business. - The effect of it
was that unless his name was immediately removed
from the black-list, he would publish a lively
chronicle of His Excellency’s after-office-hour
amusements in Melbourne. The Commissioner
retained his post.
A monument to gentle Richard Jefferies
is to be placed in Salisbury Cathedral. It will be
executed by Miss Thomas, an Australian artist,
who received her lirst training in Melbourne,
under Summers.
Dear Bulletin, —An official report from the
expensive pen of Chief Commissioner Speight
has ruthlessly burst up the respectable fiction
that the Victorian Railways were “paying.”
Of recent years it has been customary with
Duncan Gillies to wave las fat political
paw in the direction of the nearest engine
and request the country at large to heap blessings
on his head for maintaining a system which was
innocently supposed to be run on the strictest com-
mercial principles, as commercial principles are
understood by a public which buys candles
at threepence farthing and sells them atfourpence
halfpenny. Speight, again, would greedily
drink in the cheers of the assembled banqueters
when he stood up to announce that the commis-
sioners, regardless of parliamentary intrigues
and insidious back - stairs influences, were
pledged to make the railways pay. Daily
newspapers greeted the issue of each fresh
loan with a complacent snuffle on the subject of
reproductive works in the direction of new lines.
The periodical recurrence of accidents, followed
by gorgeous grants to the widows and the father-
less—not to mention the city gentlemen whose silk
hats and self-composure had been damaged in the
collision —were regarded as mere spots upon the
genial sun of railway profits. The bubble lasted
much longer than Gillies’ mythical Budget sur-
plus, which was pricked in the very infancy
of its prismatic splendour. In fact, the
annual profits on the Victorian railway traffic
had become almost an integral portion of the
colony’s swelling prosperity when Speight
issued his report. “It is not possible under the
present policy of railway construction for the net
revenue from railways to meet the interest on the
loan-moneys expended.” In a lightsome, ex-
hilarating spirit the report goes on to prattle
about the “prospective value” of the con-
cern, and to suggest that the due payment of
interest on capital from profits on business may
be held over until such profits are real in-
stead of bogus. Meanwhile, the glorious
reproductive future of the “system” is
pegging backwards a lot faster than the
years x’oll on. In 1880 there was not a poli-
tician or engine-greaser in Victoria who didn’t
bulge out with patriotic pride at the thought of
what the railways would be doing in ten years’
time. At the very lowest estimate they were to
be returning eight per cent, interest on the invest-
ment. It unfortunately happened, however, that
in those ten years the proportion of gross receipts
swallowed up by working expenses has risen
exactly eight per cent., and the nett earnings on
capital expended have practically decreased. The
inevitable result of which has been a sinful raising
of the commissioners’ salaries and a steady growth
of cussedness on the condition of the noble army
of porters. -F.E. [As the primary object of all
communal institutions is ncff the realisation of
profit, but the increase of the community's comfort,
so long as State money is not absolutely frittered
away, the failure of the. Victorian railways to pay
a dividend is not a cause for national sorrow.
Profits thus reaped, simply mean the passing of
money from one pocket to another, —Ed. B.]

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, July 30,1890.
My Dear Moorabixda,—
That ultra-Cohservative institution, Sydney University*
is developing an intensely Radical yearning to see how its
doings look in print that would make the old dons rustle in
their shrouds. The latest amendments (moved and carried by
Mr. Teece and Professor Gurney) are that copies of each
prize poem or essay be sent to the editors of the “ leading ”
(meaning Conservative) Sydney newspapers, and that
copies of the said literary efforts shall be printed for
publication—the ’Varsity to “ stand Sam,” as the bad boys
say. In other words, the poems are to appear as adver-
tisements. To which Sappho subscribes her gracious
approval. Who knows what gems of thought hav e been
wasted on an aggregate of bored pundits, tired to death
with pronouncing on the merits of miles of screed,
and grunting superciliously over even the prize-
copy? The disheartened though victorious grad, up
to the present has meekly received his medal and MSS.,
probably pawning the first and giving the latter to
his best girl who curled her front hair with it and
was happy, and there the matter lapsed, as the lawyers
say. Now the prize-writings will be immortalised, and
even although graduate prize poetry is of the “And here
the proud Ulysses laughed aloud ” order, and the prose a
swell of polished periods where there isn’t a word under
three syllables long and an idea less than 800 years old-
still the budding savant has his moments—and even if he
does get preserved for no adequate literary reason his
inspiration will come in handy to wrap up the butter of a
(nocking posterity. So far as I have been able to ascertain
the verse with the poetry in it does not come from the
University so often as it comes from tire son of the
washerwoman who lives at Jamheroo—or from the boy
who was brought up on a selection and can’t spell.
In honour of Sir Charles and Lady Halle, Sydney Lieder-
tafel issued cards for a conversazione in the Town Hall
vestibule on 24th ult., when a large, cold aggregate
arrived and occupied themselves in dodging the able
bodied zephyrs that blew on every side from 8 p in. till nine
o’clock, at which time the guests of the evening dropped
into the arm-chairs provided'for them, and the business
began. The Liedertafel, Miss Saville, Mrs. Vanderveer
Greene, Kowalski, and Lemmone (it was significant that
ho local fiddler turned up to ply the bow for Lady Halle’s
edification)—all did their best with their locks blown on
end and a devastating echo making havoc of their
top notes. Lady Hall 6 wore a scarlet cloak lined
with ermine, her gown being of black velvet, laced up the
back and relieved with maize and jet; black plume Ran
diamonds. Mayoress Burdekin’s dress was black net lace
draped over ecru satin, diamond ornaments and white wrap;
Mrs. Vanderveer Green (who received a lovely basket of
flowers after singing in liquid tones, “My heart and thy
sweet voipe ”) wore white satin and gold-spangled gauze
capote of pink roses ; Miss Saville (the recipient of two
bouquets) wore a pansy-blossom costume in, yellow satin
and crystal net; Miss Broomfield, cream silk and emerald-
green velvet bows; Mrs. Daniells, yellow satin and
Maltese lace ; while a lady created a mild sensation in a
scarlet satin tea-gown with Watteau train and voluminous
folds of coarse lace and a large, red feather as a peri-
cranial improvement ! Excellent refreshments were set
out in the Mayor’s room at the conclusion of the concert,
“ after which the proceedings were of a social character.”
The Liedertafel’s own little efforts were greatly appre-
ciated, and if only the tenors weren’t overbalanced by the
other voices the society would leave nothing to be desired
vocally.
Angry anti-Ibsemtes ana exalted ultra-Ibsenites (by the
by, some nasty people call them “ obscenitcs ”) wage a
war in the dailies over the moral issues touched upon in a
“ Doll’s House,” and a lady ably spreads herself over a lot
ofS.M. Herald space to show that Nora only did what
every ideal married woman yearns to do every day. But
yours very truly is of opinion that, to the typical
female, bound about by a cherished conventionality,
“appearances ” arc a lot dearer than morals, and even if
she had insight enough to discover that her husband was
a metaphysical alien to her, that wouldn’t worry her very
deeply so long as society knew their union was strictly
legal, whereas she would revolt from a socially-illcgal
union with a man whose thoughts were her’s. Ibsenites
argue frantically against conventionality as being woman’s
chiefest manacle ; but who forges the fetters fastest—men
or women? Mrs. Grundy is a female who finds her chief
joy in setting up high social hurdles that bring you a
damaging social cropper if you can’t clear without tap-
ping ; and if you insist upon taking away her obstacles,
you might as well do away with herself. Put a woman in
a world where “ How very improper !” was an obsolete
expression, and she would wither and wilt in a fortnight;
and a tea-swilling female confronted with a brand-new
social condition under which pretty Mrs. A could leave Mr.
A. at any moment for the most improvable reason, and
give rise to no blighting scandal, would burst on the pre-
mises from corked-up innuendo And as to whether
woman are dominated by then yearning soulfulness,
possibly not one she in a million acts on spiritual impulse
after she is 20 and begins to see a glimmer of the hard
realities of life. Ibsen makes Nora a girl probably be-
cause he knew that after girlhood the average lantern-
jawed, hdugry-eyed woman wouldn’t care a snap about
George’s mental attitude so long us she kept a maid and
had a maximum of bonnets per annum—simply because
she prefers three meals a day in the hand to an unspecified
number of hungry outcast exalted up-reachings in the bush
—and small blame to her either. Ibsen’s theories may be
put into practice by women who gain materially in
shunting the “old man”—that is, after the old man
doesn’t exert his authority and chain her up to the bed-
post as she is about to take a theatrical farewell, but that
the wage-earning Benedict may not fear such a dereliction
of wifely duty, Sappho will wager a hat. By the way.
what a field it opens up for the latter-day Phryne who
could conveniently discover that “spiritual union” was
impossible with No. 1, and then reach out smilingly (or
another alter ego !
It rained assorted animals on the evening of 26th inst.,
when the joyous “ Paul Jones ” was romped through under
the critical eyes of Lord and Lady Carington, Miss Walenn
and Oapt. Davis, R.N., with Brother Rupert and Capt.
Helbert to keep the draught off them at the back. Lady
Carington was charming in a bodice and train of ruby
satin brocaded in grey, the jupe being composed of soft
cream lace, which also formed the sleeves, front and neck
folds of the low bodice—the sleeves reaching to the elbow
and caught across with bands of the brocade; a pearl
necklet with a diamond clasp, diamond brooches, bangles
and aigrettes - the latter appearing in the becoming head-
dress of ruby Velvet, with a large tarantula bow on the
left side—white gloves and a gauze fan. The managerial
bouquet was wisely chosen in pale neutral tints—
always safe when the colour of the gown is not known.
“ Paul Jones ” improves with age, and Our Nellie (who
now wears a ripping black and pink gown in the last act)
dances her pas se'ul with a vigour that reveals the full
secret of the “biff ” underskirt even to the last row of
Hie circle, who snatch at their glasses and don’t know what
to t hink of it. And in the ballet, in act two, when those
spidery danseuses—all sadly lacking in the bran and saw-
dust of artifice—flop backwards on one shank with the
other straight out behind like a railway signal, their
adorers in the stage-boxes have to shrink against the wall
while the pink and blue satin slippers jerk wildly within a
hair of their noses. Nobody seems to mind, however.
At the Athenaeum Club dinner to Sir Charles Halid, the
other day, the inspired litterateur who evolved the menu
spelled rotis —“ rots.” It was thereupon unanimously
decided that faultless fact is an uncalled-for virtue on a
hill of fare.
A successful children’s dance was presided over by Miss
Ruperta Carington at Government House on her birthday
(24th ult.), when the festivities commenced at the wise
hour of half-past four. Professor Anderson gave a con-
juring exhibition in the ball-room, which was beautifully
decorated, after which a rekerky “ tea” was partaken of,
followed by dancing—the three little hostesses joining in
the Spanish and other dances with the enthusiasm which
is a refreshing feature in the vice-regal infants—who
were all frocked alike in dainty white gowns and blue
sashes.
A very quiet wedding—quiet probably on account of
the difference of creed between the high contracting
parties—was that of Miss Lilian Salomons, eldest daughter
of Mr. Julian Salomons, Q.C., of “ Sherbourne,” Wool-
lahra, and Mr. George Simpson, only son of Attorney-
General Simpson, of Sydney, which took place on 18th
July at St. Mark’s, Darling Point. The bride wore a
dainty walking dress of white camel’s hair cloth and rep
silk with hat en suite, and carried a charming posy of
white roses, snowdrops, violets, and maiden-hair tied with
broad satin ribbons. She had only two bridesmaids.
A small civic function took place behind Sydney Town
Hall on 24th ultimo, when Mayoress Burdekm made a
few pats at the foundation-stone of the new electric-light
engine-house, which .is to he quite an edifice, and is to
cost the tired taxpayers £4150. Mrs. Burdekin wore a
perfectly charming gown in mignonette-green Russell
cord, with panel designed in large green velvet arum lily
leaves; the designed material appearing on the bodice
in a kind of Zouave outlined in dark green silk tiny pom.
poms which also depended from the cuffs. Empire cravat
in tan Maltese lace and bonnet in (awn and copper-red
velvet with autumn leaves and crocus-buds, parasol in
fawn crepe lisse—which rather spoiled the ensemble to my
opinion. There were a number of other smart costumes.
After the sorrowful ceremony (and the laying of founda-
tion-stones always gives me the jumps—you see the
papers and coins buried in the presence of living people
who will be mouldering skeletons long ere the disinter-
ment of the hidden records) the guests climbed up to the
Mayor’s room, where refreshments were served, under the
benign influence of Town Clerk Daniels. In the afternoon
Mrs. Burdekin opened the Dog Show at the Exhibition.
At the crowded Hall 6 morning concert on 23rd ult., the
most notable feature was the shrill squeal of the dis-
appointed matinee fiend relieving her feelings on the
steps by reiterating a crescendo intention of “getting her
money back ”—a harsh material objection that didn’t fit
in with the opening arpeggios of the “ Moonlight" sonata.
Lady Carington wore shot bronze and pink silk with
folded bodice with braces and ridged sleeves of bronze
velvet, bonnet in gold straw with bronze velvet bows,
wings and blush roses; the three children were in white
frocks, pink sashes, and drawn brown silk hats.
Snazelle was resplendent in a curry-coloured get-up
reminiscent of a mild bilious attack, and a charming
creature was confident in a gas-green garment, buttercup
boots and a red sunshade. Lady Hal 16 wore an artistic
tea-gown in ash-grey chiffon, the demi-trained skirt in
accordeon pleats and broidered with a band of dark-grey
velvet design; the stole front and flowing sleeves in the
chiffon; Zouave and sleevelets in grey velvet cut in
turrets at the back and broidered in gold; the fronts
designed in gold; two long ends of grey figured ribbon
depending from the waist in front, grey shoes and
diamond ornaments. I think the chiffon sleeves of
this dress were a mistake viewed from a musical stand-
point—they waved in a distracting manner on to the
bridge of the violin and took one’s eye off the melody, so
to speak—thus jerked her audience back from Mozart to
millinery. theconcert was a symmetrical
success.
The Hall 6 farewell matinee concert at the Centenary
Hall on 28th inst. was attended by the same old throng
with nothing special on, and the same stereotyped expres-
sion of rapture interjected in the wrong place. Lady
Carington arrived rather late, attended by brother Rupert
and Miss Walenn, and was arrayed in light sage-green
cashmere, with basque bodice and sleeves covered with
close patterned black lace, pleated vest and revers ter-
minating at the shoulder in perky epaulettes in the green,
yellow gloves, a dainty bonnet in dark-green velvet add
ivy leaves and a dark-brown velvet mantollete edged with
brown fur. Ecru muslin and lace with crescendo of
crepe lisse and diamond bangles and rings was Neruda’s
airy attire on a day that chilled the marrow in one’s bones.
At the commencement of the second part a nervous gen-
tleman, with elephant’s-breath-coloured hair, appeared
on the stage and presented Lady Halid with a silver lyre-
bird with a gold-and-silver tail, roosting on a mahogany
pedestal which bore the usual cofflnish inscription, all of
which was a “ gift from a few of the—hum—professional
musicians of Sydney or—ah—as a token of their—ahem—
esteem . . . superb talent . . . advantage of
hearing you,” &c., at which outburst of professional
enthusiasm Lady Hall 6 blushed like a school-girl, but
unfortunately did not inform the company that she was
“ too overwhelmed to thank them ” in good, old, sobbing-
tones —probably because that was about how she really
did feel. The llall6s left for Melbourne by express on
29th ult.

The vice-regal inspection of Fort-street Public-school
on 25th ult., was a very successful ordeal—the infants
presenting Lady Carington with an address for her
youngest daughter, at the reception of which Lady C.
was“ visibly affected,” and of which the Baron remarked
that when lie got back to his own old, grey home that
address would he bricked into the w all of the front hall
“ as perhaps the most cherished object in the house.”
After the cantata, march past, and the speeches, luncheon,
cooked by the girls in the school kitchen, was partaken of
by the vice-regal party, after which Lady Carington in-
spected the needlework, and the proceedings terminated.
Lady Carington, in a short and pretty speech, signified
her intention of bringing Miss Judith Carington to
“show' her thanks in her own childish way” to the
school-children.
At the inspection of the Sydney public-school cadets,
on Moore Park, Lady Carington will present colours to
the juvenile regiment. The vice-regal party will include
Gov. Hamilton, of Tasmania, with Lady Hamilton and
suite, who will arrive at Government House about
18th Aug.

for the purpose of opening the Waterworks in'that cold
and frosty district. They will stay two days—the first to

picnic among the lotty Canoblas—let us hope a westerly
won’t be blowing at the time.

The stray cow nuisance is still to the fore. It is most

And the Echo, the organ of “wit and humour
without cynicism and scurrility,” gives us this,
also in long, primer : .
I see from the papers that Madame Melba achieved a
great success in the role of Ophelia in “ Hamlet,” in
French, at‘Covent Garden. It is to be hoped that she
will not get a surfeit by over-indulgence in “French
rolls.’
One of the most remarkable escapes ever
made from Pentridgo took place, some years ago,
and even Pierre Douar’s artistic flight is left in the
shade by comparison. A gang of convicts were
engaged in erecting a massive wall round the grey,
old prison, when one ingenious person persuaded
his mates to build him into the wall. A few air-
holes were left in the mortar, and that night
87028, or whatever his official designation might
be, lay hidden in the solid masonry, while the in-
dignant warders were wearing themselves to
tatters rushing over the surrounding country in
pursuit of him. When darkness came on 87028
gave a tremendous heave which shifted the block
of stone above his hiding place, and emerged from
his concealment, and fled. The language dis-
charged after him next morning, when the scheme
was discovered, was too powerful for publication,
but he never came back.
A plain, rowdy-looking bushman lately
dropped in at a Queensland police-court and sat
down to watch the administration of justice.
Australian justice, however, is an article that
seldom leaves a favourable impression on the
beholder and the visitor contained himself with
difficulty until a contempt of court case occurred
and the prisoner was fined in the sum of 21s. Then
the visitor went softly up and deposited the sum
of 425. with the clerk, stating that ho felt at least
twice as contemptuous as the man who had just
gone out, and that if he had had any more money
about him he would have let his feelings out and
indulged right off in £5 worth of derision for that
court and everybody connected with it. Owing to
financial pressure, however, he would subdue the
rest of his sentiments till he got his next cheque,
and then he would come down and let fly all the
disgust that was in him.
In China all outstanding debts become
null and void at the end of the year, and the
sheventy-per-shent Chow who can’t overtake his
debtor by five minutes to twelve on the last night
of the year may as well give up. Consequently
the few days before that fatal date are filled with
invoices, and letters of remonstrance, and duns,
and clamour, and every Mongol who has a cent
owing to him is plunging round in search of the
dishonest pagan who objects to pay. As a rule
he seldom finds him, for he is hidden away some-
where, and won’t emerge till he feels himself safe'.
But as pretty nearly everybody owes money to
somebody else, and none of them ever settle-up if
they can help it, China comes out about square.
Nobody has got his own money, but everybody
who is not a congenital idiot has got somebody
else’s money instead. The custom is one that wo
strongly recommend for adoption in Australia, as
being better fitted for exportation than any other
Chinese custom we ever heard of.

“Frou-Frou,” as played at the Sydney
Criterion, differs greatly, if memory be faithful,
from the version first presented to an Eng-
lish-speaking public with Mademoiselle Beatrice,
we imagine, in the name-part. Comparison
is not wholly to the advantage of Charles
Chairington’s adaptation, for the introductory
acts are “ talky,” and the concluding situations of
the two last acts, strong though they be, are
dragged out to a length that sorely tries not only
the actress on whose shoulders the burden of re-
presentation mainly falls, but also the capacity of
■a mixed audience for sustaining extreme tension
without lapsing into a revulsionary condition. The
performance of Janet Aohurch is not so satisfy-
ing as her Nora. In the lighter scenes
up to the close of the third act—here the jealous
denunciation of her sister and frantic-exit are per-
fect— natural and equal to every emergency, her
methods in the concluding acts suggest a replica of
some French original. Miss Achnrch has already
shown that she need not make use of models for
her inspiration : she has the application, intelli-
gence and emotional sensibility necessary for self-
imbuement with a foreign personality. Her
studies, therefore, should be from the life only, for
the performer who thinks and feels before simu-
lating has only to reconcile two personalities,
while the mere imitator has to blend the conflict-
ing natures of at least three, an assimila-
tion that always suggests artificiality even to
auditors who are unable to discover the
reason for their convictions. Miss Achnrch can,
and will, improve on her present performance;
but, even as it stands, no actress in Australia
could approach within measurable distance of it,
Sartorys, in the hands of Mr. Titlieradge,
scarcely has the weight and dignity that should
be imparted to it, but is otherwise faultless. In
the last act, when his pent-up love for the erring
wife sweeps away the barriers set up by pride, his
thrilling “ Gilberte” is a particularly fine touch.
Next in importance is the performance of Louise
by Fanny Enson—admirable throughout, one of the
very best things of the evening is the manner in
which she receives the proposal of Sartorys—whom
she loves—for the hand of Gilberte. Herbert
Flemming’s Valreas is flawless, and now that Chas.
Charrington has settled down in a wig that does
not wholly outrage possibility, his impersonation
of the giddy father betokens careful and intelligent
study. Minor characters are well interpreted by
Nita Steele, whose last appearance on these-
boards, perhaps in Sydney, was with Essie Jenyns:
by our very old friend J. P. West, and Alice May,
while “ Bob ” Watson, having escaped safely from
“Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” scores heavily in a “one
side ” part. Hedley Churchward’s Sartorys’
interior is a delicate and artistic piece of colouring,
and liis backcloth picture of a Venetian lagoon
affords further proof of his versatility. By the
way, “A Doll’s House” was taken off in the
height of its popularity ; it would pay, and please
the profession, who ate dying to see it, should
management revive it at a matinee or two.

“ Paul Jones ” is drawing big biz at Syd-
ney Royal. The feature of the show is the singing
of a carefully-trained chorus of fresh voices, one
of the best that ever came hitherward. Aggie
Kelton’s sabot song is the surest encore of the
performance; the little woman’s voice seems
thinner than of yore, but she is as kittenish as
ever. When Snazelle draws hia cutlass out of
Leitch’s tobacco-pouch now, he wipes off at least
a pint of gore (property-made) which runs down
towards the footlights with a realism that would
gratify the soul of a carcase-butcher.
* *: m *
Maggie Moore has caught on to some pur-
pose at Her Majesty's, where big houses prevail.
George Rignold has worked a couple of new
situations into “ Meg,” and that flop-footed speci-
men of dramatic workmanship does not limp so
confoundedly as on the first nights of production.
Maggie Moore has introduced another song or
two, but Harry Jewett’s refreshments are viewed
with growing incredulity by the gallery, whose
knowledge of ont-door refreshments entitle them
to speak with authority on the subject.

A Cyclorama advertisement says : “ Listen
to the thunder of the cannon and the voice of the
bugle on the battlefield. Hear. Heaven's artillery
and see the lightning play about the forms of
fighting thousands at Gettysburg.” We won’t
give the thing away, we will not let the public
know that the noise of “Heaven’s artillery” is
produced by the janitor sitting down suddenly
on an upturned weak-bottomed tin-tub, that the
flashes of “ lb even s lightning ’’are evoked by the
band’s lighting his pipe inside the rim of an
ophicle’de, and the rain effects by the opera-glass
boy dropping marbles into General Meade’s helmet.
Oh, no, we wouldn't give the thing away !
