The Kev. Charles Clark concludes his
School of Arts season on Thursday evening with
the Thackeray lecture, which, on a prior occasion
(much to the astonishment of the Much-Travelled),
attracted one of the largest houses of the season.
The lecturer is apparently a hero-worshipper : he
never speaks in anything but the highest terms of
the great men who have so unconsciously been his
benefactors. The peccadilloes of Thackeray and
Dickens, like the Irish and Scotch atrocities of
Cromwell the Great, arc drowned in the Lethe of
forgetfulness, and the deeds that made them famous
are depicted in the most glowing and warranted-
never-to- wash-out colours. Perhaps the lecturer is
right • perhaps it is best to ignore evil when no good
can be done by stirring it up. Anyhow, the
“Thackeray ” decture is one of Mr. Clark’s best
efforts, and his description of the manner in which
the Ponto’s governess played “Sicha gittin’ up
Stairs ” would bring smiles to the face of a stone-
god Sydney School of Arts having proved much
too "small for Rev. Charles Clarke’s audiences, he
moves next week to the elegant Centenary Hall,
commencing on Thursday.
“ Formosa,” at Melbourne Royal, will do
Kttle to restore the ruddy glow of health to the
wan check of a tired management. A reprehen-
sible spirit of contrariness seems to hover around
the present revival, making most of the principals
do things they ought not to do, in the wrong way,
and filling listless liandfnlls of supers, with a fatal
sense of their limited numbers at the very moments
when they should ho descending upon the chief
situations with au irresistible swoop. There is no
artistic force in the production, so it happens that
Boucicault’s cynical contempt for sporting possi-
bilities stands revealed in all its preposterous
nakedness, and the incidents which never were,
and could never he, stick out from the show like
finger-posts pointing the thumb of scorn at their
own inanity. At this time of day it is barely
worth while to mention, once again, that “For-
mosa ” is a reckless outrage upon all rules of boat-
racing, and that the heroic Oxford stroke-oar who
trained for a University contest in the arms of
Delilah, and got down to his proper rowing weight
on a course of baccarat and lobster-salad, would
never enjoy the triumph of being number eight
in the winning crew that spurted home to Mort-
lake on the end of a string. The notion of the
gallant Oxonians going back in the betting for the
want of a leader to take them out for their daily
practice, and being driven to send the coxswain
to wait upon that lost sheep in the gilded haunts
of sin and implore him to return to headquarters
at Putney—such a notion requires acting of the
highest order to pull it through. As regards the
dual existence of Jenny Boker, who occasionally
tears herself away from the caresses of a dissolute
nobility to enjoy the virtuous calm of her parents’
rural pub. —there is less room for ridicule. Women
were deceivers ever, and even a “tawny-haired
syren of Hyde Park ” would make some occasional
sacrifices of lordly endearments for the pleasure of
making a fool of her chuckle-headed mother.
Moreover, Miss Formosa Boker does comparatively
respectable madiing down at the pub., by which
means she probably contrives to keep herself sup-
plied with a sort of modest blush, and a faint
semblance of a maidenly twitter.
# # *
The many acting opportunities which this
naughty old drama affords are pretty generally
misused at the Royal. Miss Edith Blande is no
more than a negative success as Formosa. Critics
who cannot distinguish between a fine part and a
fine performance, and who have never seen this
particular character more completely filled, may
bo satisfied with Miss Blande’s interpretation.
Yet it lacks tenderness in the one place and dash
in the other. Miss Maud Williamson has to
squander her graceful presence upon an insipid
secondary character, and pretty Miss Gwynne is
as nice as she will ever he until she learns
to hold herself straight, it makes one sad to
see so winning a creature always suffering
from nervous contortion. Hoyce is excellent in an
original rendering of the man who wants to sell a
little dawg. Clyndes is rather mechanical and
tamo in Irving’s old part, and Captain Do Burgh
altogether out as Major somebody, who should he
the brightest and most popular member of the
crowd. A really capable comedian, in Fred
Neebe, converts the pathetic figure of Dr. Doremus
into a solemn caricature of Ally Sloper, while the
Boker’s ph'c and mere are badly misrepresented by
George Walton and Mrs. Walter Hill. The latter
merely serves as a noisy reminder that the firm
should import at least a collide of clever “old
women” actresses as soon as possible. Others of
the company are passable, especially Athol wood
in a very difficult position, and the scene of For-
mosa’s villa at Fulham is a tine florid work of
Brunton’s art. A little improvement on the first
night’s performance will enable the drama to run
three weeks.
There was another crowded house at Mel-
bourne Bijou, on Saturday, and “Turned Up,”
admirably played and staged, will probably do as
good business as the “Pickpocket” did for one
week only. Next Saturday (Aug. 2) the B. and B.
talent will be let loose upon “The Balloon ” and
“Kerry,” a double bill which should render
another rapid change unnecessary. “ The Bal-
loon ” has not yet made its ascent in Melbourne,
while “Kerry ” never fails to strike home.

Two other theatres break out afresh upon
the same evening. Although good houses have
sighed over the costly love-story of Bellew and
Mrs. Potter, and the fame of the balcony scene
has spread afar, yet Sardou is about to he substi-
tuted for Shakespeare. They will return to “La
Toscjj, ” for six nights before trying “ Frou-Frou,”
which latter should last out the remainder of the
present season, until such time as the comic opera
company return from Adelaide. At the Opera
House, where business has got down to zero,
Saturday is to witness a revival of “ Held by the
Enemy,” with “a powerfully augmented east.”
And no Melbourne east is in such desperate need
of augmentation as that which has hitherto hung
like a mill-stone around William Rignold’s
massive neck.

“ Master and Man '’ fan act of which went
off with a loud report at Dr. Neild’s matinee) is
good for another week at the Alexandra. Last
(Wednesday) night the Melbourne Trades Council
were to sit around the dress-cirole and beam upon
the virtue of the labouring man as contrasted with
the abnormal vice of a master who seduces the
wives of them that earn his gold by the sweat of
their brows, and otherwise indicate that capital
is antagonistic to the just rights of the working-
man.
** * *
Slade Murray and his rather ambitious
variety show are fetching them in overflowing
multitudes to the Gaiety Hall; the Victoria ap-
pears to be temporarily knocked out of time_; and
the Phonograph has come down to the inevitable
shilling for admission. After another week this
remarkable, but disappointing, machine will com-
mence to travel round the country, under the all-
important management of the MacMahons, whose
name has been so intimately associated with the
Phonograph in the columns of the daily press that
the world is gradually learning to accept those
immortal brethren as the inventors. Every time,
too, that the funnel breathes forth a tune, the
Macs, smile in such a superior manner and seem to
know such a lot about it that the genjus of Edison
sinks into insignificance, and his share in the
entertainment fades clean out of memory.
** # *
To-night (Thursday) the Halles start six
farewell concerts at Melbourne Town Hall, with
Miss Frances Saville as vocalist, and the Governor
present to pretend that he takes a delight in num-
bers, many of which are neither more nor less than
instruction-lessons for students of classical music,
being absolutely devoid of real meaning for nine-
tenths of the congregation.

The news that Cedric Burnett, the original
of “Little Lord Fanntleroy,” is dying of con-
sumption, fills one with a sickly sense of sorrow,
quite unmingled with surprise. No actual exist-
ing boy, bearing a mental or physical resemblance
to the hero of Mrs. Burnett’s novel, could be ex-
pected to go on actually breathing for very long.
The strain of continually calling his mother
“Dearest,” and smiling sweet, childlike grins at
an aged peer of the realm, would naturally be too
much for a constitution enfeebled by never having
a fight with the boy next door, or throwing heavy
pieces of road-metal at the police. Then, again
MR. GEORGE LEITCH.
if the original Cedric is burdened with a bustle
only distantly approaching to those of bis stage
representatives, he must of necessity die young.
He isn’t built the right way to enable him to
effect escapes from his tough, unfeeling comrades,
when they arrange to dowse him in the water-butt
and comb out bis long, dank, golden tresses with a
garden-rake. No doubt the other bays have
amused themselves by wetting Cedric right through
until the ,cold settled on his lungs and laid the
seeds of that distressing complaint which is slowly
passing him hence. Perhaps he used to protest
that he would run home and tell “Dearest” how
unkind they were, and such conduct would inevi
tably hasten his end considerably. This world
was never intended for Little Lord Fanntleroy,
or else Providence would not have invented
hair-cutters ; and if any gentleman present
has a large blue-eyed son who persists in
calling his mamma “Dearest’ after he (the
blue-eyed innocent) has turned seven, the
said gentleman is earnestly recommended to join a
Benefit Burial Society, iu readiness for the sad
family bereavement that must shortly come about.
The writer had a kind of Fanntleroy boy in train-
ing for some few years, and used to watch the
kid’s glorious bead of hair glinting in the sunlight,
and the broad lace collar flapping round bis snow-
white neck. It was all right until the pride of
his fond parents began to pine and wither under the
influence of a spiritual asthma and the weight
of his lovely wool. Then a practical-minded
dearest lopped-off the clustering curls and turned
the damask-cheeked treasure loose among the
beasts of the field, and the bad, unwashed boys
who play marbles, until Fanntleroy left off
coughing and began to mutilate his garments
something dreadful, and otherwise lay the founda-
tion of a happy, commonplace, bald-headed old
age.

The Turners, who made their first appeal*

The British drama is not to be allowed to
play a lone hand out here any longer; it is being
taken in hand with a view to improvement.
Since bis installation as manager at a certain
big theatre a well-known author-actor has care-
fully revised and considerably improved most of
the plays produced. It would be very interesting
to hear what the late Charles Reade, who could
call a spade a spade better than most men, would
have to say to this,

Patriotism is sometimes carried a little too
far. When the foundation-stone of Sydney Town
Hall engine-house was laid by Mrs. Burdekin,
the Mayor ventured to declare that the Sydney
Town Hall was “the finest in the world.” Evi-
dently he has not beard of —much less seen —the
Hotel de Yille in Paris, or the Town Halls of
Berlin and Vienna.

Williamson cannot persuade the Kendals
to come, except on Fatti-like terms, of mopping
up all the cash. We’ll get them grandfatherly
and grandmotherly, like the Keans, when they
are a pair of bolsters, slobbering each other over
on the boards. Still the critics will gush over the
rheum, arcl find that this visit is most opportune
to show our young Australian slobberers how to
do it—or how not.

Mr. George Leitch, now recovered from a
serious illness induced by a stage-accident, attri-
butes his recovery greatly to the freedom from the
cares of management that followed bis joining the
Nellie Stewart Opera Company. It is some time
since Mr. Leitch appeared in Sydney, and he now
shows his versatility by raising Pierre—a mere
shred of a character, and altogether at variance
with his Prince Malleotti in “ Forget me-Not,”
Judge Thibbets in “The Pearl Diver,” Parson
Meekin in “ His Natural Life,” and Walter Chis-
holm in a. “ Touch of the Sun ’’-—into prominence.
As an author Mr. Leitch (George Ralph Walker)
has shown sufficient ability toensure the production
of “UncleZac.,” “Sithors toGrind,” “A Touch of
the Sun,” “The Improvisators,” “The Coming D,”
“Money Down,” “(Id Times,” “Those Girls”
on the British stage and in the colonies, “ The
Pearl Diver,” “Wanda” (written for the
Maierqnis), “ The Librarian ” (a free translation
of Yon Moser’s “ Der Bibliotheka,” also the
source of “The Private Secretary”), and an
adaptation of “ His Natural Life,” which book he
was the first dramatist to approach. Mr. Leitch
has also collaborated with Mr. Walter Besant in
“The Reigning Beauty,” a play founded on one
of that popular novelist’s works, and now in
Beerbohm Tree’s hands, who will, in all proba-
bility, ere long stage it at the Haymai’ket.
* * * *
A writer iu the World observes that “ no
actor-manager ever gave full play to his powers
through his doing only the tilings he liked best to
do, always following the line of least resistance, and
living his whole artistic life, so to speak, with the
limelight on his face.” This may bo read as a
solemn warning to Handsome George, who prefers
to play maudlin heroes, instead of artistic villains,
merely for the joy of wearing a wearisome halo at
the end of every act. George Darrell, for like
reason, has for years been making love instead of
representing semi-comic friends of the family,
parts in which he would be always seen to advan-
vantage. Dampier, again, is not entirely free
from a similar weakness. Fortunately there are
no limelight considerations to influence Brough
and Boucicault in their choice of characters, while
as for Williamson and Garner, they seem to follow
the line of least resistance by not acting at all.

“Pendragon” writes in the London
Referee :—-
A friend, who usually knows his way about, asked me
the other day: “How is it that theatrical managers
(I note in their puff advertisements 1 Opinions of the Press’
from all sorts of rum and queer and shy papers, and
papers which no one ever se.es and never hears of, except
under these circumstances, while it is a rare thing to see
the' Referee quoted ?” This was not a very easy question
to answer, as most folk who happen to be well up in the
ways of mummers and employers of mummers will under-
stand. Fortunately, as T have said, my friend usually
knows his way about, and it didn’t take long for him to
learn that only stuff is quoted that is not critical—or that ,
being critical,'i»of a kind which fits in with a manager’s
view of what atone should constitute criticism. “Ah,”
slid lie, “it's easy enough to see it now; but 1 never
thought of it like that before , and knowing how your
paper is run after by play-actors as well as playgoers,
I've often wondered how it is you’re not, as a rule, starred
in the advertisement columns of the daily papers. Dear
me 1 And yet it’s as plain as the nose on Henry Petit fa
face if only you come to think about it.” Perhaps my
friend would have been still more emphatic if he’d known
all I know about the way in which some managers as good
as demand favourable notices; and how when they do
quote from a real good paper they take a line from here
and a sentence from there, and make by this means what
is really an awful slate appear quite complimentary. The
publio.is always being treated as if it were the most con-
summate of asses; and it really must be such if it can
swallow some of the “opinions* of the Press” that have
been put forth of late by certain “ eminent ” theatrical
lessees and proprietors.
The above paragraph applies quite as well to The
Bulletin in Australia as to the rad’eal Referee
in London.
#* * *
J. L. Toole is given to informing the
snobocracy of Australia, in strictest confidence, of
his intention to speak to his friend the Prince of
Wales about The Bulletin. It seems that the
first time this rather over-fed and over-rated
Johnny read the periodical in question he was
overwhelmed by its awful “ disloyalty.” Nothing
so inimical to the interests of perpetual monarchy
and the House of Lords had met his eyeglass in
the whole course of his life. He shuddered so
hard that the glass dropped from his line of vision
into the soup, and floundered helplessly among
the bits of maccaroni while Toole sat gasping out —
“Isay, yerknow, it won’t do at all, yerknow, it
will have to be stopped, I say,” and much more
eloquence of a similar nature. The Bulletin
was, in fact* a ghastly revelation to Hingland’s
greatest comedian, striking terror to his simple,
little soul. Why was such a thing permitted, he
desired to know, and when somebody mentioned,
with an apologetic cough, that it was an unavoid-
able calamity established for the purpose of seeing
that a lot of other things were not permitted,
Toole crumbled up into a heap of ruins and groaned
aloud. Week after week since then he has held
small, select meetings on each successive number
of The Bulletin, invariably inquiring of his
honourable friends whether they have seen that,
and what ought to be done in the matter. After
laying out his own sixpence on another dreadful
number he will go around buttonholing creation to
inquire why they take it. One man, being pro-
bably lost to all sense of shame, said he took it as
a tonic every Sunday morning in bed,, whereupon
poor old Johnny, who is not wanting in pluck,
fired up indignantly and asked that man if he
thought Her Majesty would read such a scandalous
publication regularly, sir. The man aforesaid ad-
mitted that His Gracious Queen would not do
anything of the kind, because the first dose would
probably up her constitution on the spot,
and Toole left him without another word. Poor
Johnny, bless his dreary wheezes ! is honest
enough in his dislike for the paper which strives to
pull down his stupid old idols. Wehave’read some-
where that Toole, sen., was toastmaster to the Lord
Mayor, or something equally elevating, so his
father’s son would naturally be trained up to pray
for the landed aristocracy. The rabid animosity
of a sassiety joker like Toole is no small compli-
ment to a respectable radical journal, and as he
tours around Australasia, finding The Bulletin
ever with him, we trust he will splutter his pro-
tests until all is blue. We have no fault to find
with him as a man, or as an actor in those good
old parts which consist so largely of baggy
breeches. In fact, the worst we know of Toole is
that he is considered funny by the Prince of
Wales, who will shortly be speaking to his royal
mother and Lord Salisbury about The Bulletin,
you understand.

Roundabout.
The wildly-gifted M‘Milkn, of thoN.S.W.
Cabinet, fired off a noble sentiment the other day,
He stated that he believed in “ working in that
.particular sphere where the real channels of the
people’s feedings lay, and where were to be found
at play those elements which ultimately found
their outlet in the political manifestations of the
people;” and if this lofty conception were only
turned upside-down, and tiie front end of it put in
the middle, and the verb moved up a little closer
to the audience,- and placed in a position where it
could be more easily seen with the naked eye, and
then the whole thing were read off b a kwai ds by a
one-eyed man standing on his head, it .would fill a
yawning void in the gigantic soul of the nation.
If Mae. had lost his subject down a deep drain at
midnight, and it had then been left lying in the
street for three weeks to be rim over by tram cars,
after which he had found the remains of it
in the gutter, and wiped them on the
tails of his frock-coat, it could hardly have
been clearer, even to the commonest under-
standing. The only possible objection that could be
raised is that the remark, when printed, is hardly
large enough to wrap a pound of butter in, and is
too heavy to be used as a kellick when our uncle
goes out fishing; but regarded as a weapon of
offence to lie sent in a dray to your enemy it fills
the bill. Mo long as the people's feelings continue
to “lay ’ in a ‘ channel ” and that channel is in
the sphere that Mr. M'Millan speak's about, and
provided Mr. M'Millan keeps in the same sphere
anil yet doesn t fall head-first into the channel,
and at the same time the “elements” already men-
tioned continue to find the outlet hereinbefore
alluded to, so as to avoid wear and tear of the
channel; and supposing the sphere doesn't get
damaged, and the feelings which are lying in the
channel don’t come out and go somewhere els -,
there doesn’t seem to be much danger.

The latest report concerning Ned Trickett.
once the god of Australia’s idolatry, is that he lias
applied for a 40-acre lease, and is going out to look
for silver. It is hardly worth while being a cham-
pion sculler at all if the end is to be such a woeful
fall as this. To clump wearily over a sodden hill-
side in search of that fleeting delusion known as
“ indications.” To dodge on all-fours into old,
disused tunnels, and crawl up heaps of ancient
t’eiris and fall violently down on the other side,
and land half-stunned in
a squashy spot, while his
mate crawls after him,
and fqlls on t p of him,
and lands his extinguished
candle in Ned’s mouth
and ear, and then to blas-
pheme at each other in
the dark, and try to
spit out the candle, and
accuse each otherof being
the cause of the disaster.
Then to get up again and
-wade along that tunnel
till they find a lode which
made everybody insol-
vent who tried to work
it, and induced one pro-
prietor to go mad, and
murder his wife, and inter
her at midnight'on a quiet
corner of the property.
J ust here, most probably,
tliere will be that un-
happy object called a
“ rise,” and Ned will give
his mate a leg-up that he
may examine it, and the
mate will ask Ned to take
care what he is shovin’
at, and by-and-bye, when
the discussion is at its
height the man who is
ascending will lose his-
hold and comedown with
a whiz and a whoop and a
scream on the summit of
ins partner’s head. Most
likely at this point in
the proceedings, they will
disagree about the geolo-
gical formation of the
disliict, and sit down
for three-quarters of an
hour in a cold puddle to
argue the matter, Ned
being of opinion that
the formation is con-
nected with something
whose name sounds like two madmen trying
to outcurse each other, and his colleague
being convinced that it owes its existence to
a geological circumstance which resembles the
yell that a lost soul might give if it sat
down on a hot coal. It is even possible that
they will call each other bad names just about
here, and each will state that the other is an
ignorant encumbrance ; but very possibly their
• attention will be called off by noticing a
package which somebody has left in the tunnel,
and they will inspect it and find it contains
i nough dynamite to blow them and the ruins
of the claim away into the upper air. It
stands to reason that they will then leave the
tunnel hurriedly and bruise themselves a good
deal in getting out; and if they afterwards resolve
to sink a shaft the ordinary cussedness of tilings
makes it nearly certain that it will fill with water
when they have got down 100 feet. Also it is
(liree chances to one that Ned's partner will
disappear at the same time, and Ned won't
know whether he is dead and anchored down at
(lie bottom of the mine, or whether he has
kipped, and it is almost a dead sure thing that
Ned's pickaxe and his coat, hat, and boots will be
away down in the bowels of the drowned-out
property. If Ned starts to fish for the lost goods
iie wou’t got a solitary bite in 48 hours, and
it is safe to prophesy that it will rain all the
lime, accompanied by TTa.il and thunder;
and at the end of that time it will be
discovered that the lease is null and void
owing to an informality, and someone will
give Mr. Trickctt notice to quit. Whether
he will assault this person and aim mud
at his eye is a point for his own exclusive con-
sideration, but anyhow the final act Of the drama
will be tliat Ned, if he has luck, will make ids
way back to his quiet hotel at Rockhampton, with
a bad cold and a rheumatic attack in both feet,
complicated wdth severe inflammation of the car.
We have always viewed the ex-champion with a
friendly eye, and for that reason we implore him
to abandon this mining business right off, We
have tried it and escaped alive, but Ned is a per-
son of abnormal dimensions, and the hundred-
weight of rock which just skimmed over our head
the day the blast went off unexpectedly would
have taken off the top part of him bodily. If he
wants excitement and change of scene the retail
prawn industry is open to him, and the fried liah
trade presents attractions which lie , has possibly
overlooked, but unless’ lie desires to .blow 'off ail
bis limbs and return as a mere stump 1 in a wheel-
barrow he will quit mihirig riowand for ever.

Ifc is hardly worth while being a lawyer in
New Zealand now. The distinction between
barristers and solicitors has been abolished for one
tiling, and it is no longer necessary for the rising
perverterof truth and justice to be articled—he
can spend Ins days in splitting mils and digging
fence posts if lie likes, and study law at night, and
go up for examination in his hob-nailed boots, and
even if he leaves a lump of mud the size of a spar-
row on the mat as he enters the examination-room
it doesn’t matter much. And now it is proposed
to do away with the .‘ general knowledge ” quali-
fication, on the ground that a man can establish an
alibi for a hog-stealer quite as well without Latin
and Greek, or any other old resurrected language,
a; with it; and that nobody cares a straw whether
he knows or does not know the names of a line of
dead kings who got drunk beneath a gilded
dome; and that as his ditty is simply to get
up and state in his native language that his client
was plugged in the eye, it doesn't matter even if
lie can’t tell how many notes a sackbut has, or
whether shawms have strings or not. And on top
of this indignity Sir George Grey has introduced
a measure providing that doctors shall give up
spending their early years in going through
deceased languages and coming out in an exhausted
and ignorant state at the other side, and should
devote the time thus saved to learning not to stir
up a patient’s inside with a fence-rail, or to heave
sixteen different medicines at him in one bottle on
tin; off-chance that one of them will hit his com-
plaint and the results of the other fifteen be found
out at the inquest; and as a preliminary step
towards this reform it is demanded that all pre-
scriptions shall be written in English in order
that the patient may not be called upon to swallow
carbolic acid and essence of pounded snake with-
out knowing it. It seems hardly worth while to
be alive when all the dignity is taken out of things
this way.

It is rumoured that Italy proposes to resume
her Abyssinian campaign, and wipe out the combus-
tible King of Eastern Africa —or burst. There is
no sadder job on earth than an Italian campaign in
Abyssinia. The local potentate calls out all his
troops and takes gentle walking exercise about the
country, and the other fellows'pound along after
him, but always find that he is just ten miles
ahead. They can’t waste the country with fire
and sword, for the country consists chiefly of
1 nicks and dust, and it wouldn’t burn even if
kerosened and then struck by lightning. They
can’t kill anybody, for there is never anybody in
sight, except now and then when a shirtless native
can be dimly descried through a telescope. Some-
times they take up a strong position somewhere,
but no man can spend his whole existence
loafing in a strong position, and the king
simply waits till they get tired of it, knowing
by experience that a position of any kind
soon gets monotonous when nobody takes any
interest in it. Occasionally they send in an ulti-
matum, but the king merely drops it on the floor
and leaves it there. He used to put things like,
these on the file, but when the file got choke-full
of ultimatums he gave it up. Now and then a
mud hamlet is captured, but there is never any-
thing in it but old bones and infection, and even
when a proclamation is issued annexing the whole
country it makes no difference, for that intern in-
able potentate is still ten miles ahead. The only
variety in the whole proceeding is when the local
monarch proves to be ten miles behind, for a
change, and busy annexing the invaders baggage,
anil then they rush back to look for him, and he
isn t there, Generally the Italians get so mad at
this stage that they annex another thousand miles
of territory and start for home, and King John
starts after them with a sudden activity that
makes them leave all their guns behind them and
bolt, after sending in another ultimatum.
* * #
A case of riotous and festive bigamy is
under consideration in England. The offender
married his first wife in 1577, and left her two
years afterwards. He doesn't know whether she
is alive or dead, and nobody else knows either.
At all events he married again in 1880, the sufferer
( his time being a vision of ethereal loveliness with
an American divorce from her first partner, which
document is said to be no good. If the other wife
was alive on this occasion, it is obvious that a
mean fraud was perpetrated on a trusting woman,
but, on the other hand, if the American divorce
was merely a piece of waste paper, the second
marriage didn’t amount to anything, and it is a
matter of little importance whether the original
lady was alive or not. Things begin to grow mixed
at this point, and they become worse later on, for
after a while the American person began to gush
with coals and broken dishes and boots instead
of with soft endearments, and her husband
fled in fear of his life, and married somebody else.
Whether he committed bigamy this time or not
seems doubtful, for the status of the third wife is
past finding out. If the first female on the list is
really deceased, and the American divorce doesn’t
count, then she is all right; but the earth has been
turned upside down and shaken in a vain search
for that early female and nobody can find her
either alive or dead, and the beauteous American
is prepared to club anyone who says her divorce
won’t hold water. Conjecture drops down dead
at this stage of the enquiry, and though from
sheer force of habit the prisoner married last year
for the fourth time nobody seems to take much
interest in the fact. The fourth wife doesn't
come in anywhere. The great difficulty, how-
ever, is how to draw up the indictment, for it
is no earthly use to accuse the piisoner of
marrying the third wife while the first was alive if
they can’t decide whether she was alive or not, or
while the second was alu\, seeing that no one
knows whether the second lady ever had any
standing at all; and though a good and solid case
might be made out of the last marriage, seeing
that the third person in the category was certainly
in existence when that event came off, yet, as the
latest wife has fled to avoid giving evidence, this
part of the scheme necessarily falls through. Pro-
bably the handiest solution of the difficulty would
be to shut the defendant in a room with all his
mothers-in-law. You always find a l l a man’s
mother-in-law.
If a Chinaman dies while being tried for
murder it is considered a sufficient evidence of his
guilt, and his son is sent to prison, or beheaded
the case is a particularly heinous one. When the
descendant is too young he is adopted by some
Government official, and carefully reared until he
is old enough for execution. As it is a matter of
considerable importance that he shouldn’t die
before the execution cones off, every care is taken
in his rearing and education, and he goes out for
a run every day in charge of a policeman or other
public functionary, so that he may perish in good
order and condition. If there is no son then the
criminal’s father or uncle or brother gets a flog-
ging. The offence is somewhere in the family, and
somebody has got to pay for it. Even the offender’s
cook is liable to be bambooed off and on for a
month or so, if there is nobody else in the house-
hold to stand the racket.

married in a hurry, the groom fearing she would

Sir Chas. Halle was once on the point of
giving up music for a brilliant position in the
foreign diplomatic Service. Halle has hob-nobbed
with Mendelssohn, Meyerbeer, Rossini, Berlioz,
Wagner, and Lablache.
Henrik Ibsen, author of “A Doll’s House,”
is 62 years of age. Ho is undemonstrative and
self-possessed, the very embodiment of repose,
makes little out of his plays, and receives a small
pension from the Norwegian Government.
“ Corner Memory” Thompson has died in
Hampstead, London. He was so called because
he once sat down and drew out of his own head a
correct map of a large portion of London, with all
the owners of the corner houses marked down.
A few weeks ago Saunderson, M.P., an
Ulster member, called Labouchere “a gargoyle”
in Parliament. In the lobby Labby said to him,
“Wager you don’t know what a gargoyle is?”
“Yes, I do,” said Saunderson; “a noisy spout.”

manager, of the Bank of Adelaide, lias resigned,
and goes to England to enjoy his big fortune
made out of Broken Hill. Wilkinson has been a
faithful and successful bank officer and a most
plucky investor.
The Sydney Echo editor says his paper is
distinguished for “ wit and humour without
cynicism and scurrility.” In other words, the Echo
goes in for what Judge Darley once commended as
“wholesome fun”—the Judge’s favourite organ
whereof is now dead as Mr. Marley.
The obscene parody on Purves’s second
Christian (!) name, which nearly turned Melbourne
City Court into a prize-ring last Thursday, was
uttered by David Gaunson as a whispered “ aside,”
but a second of perfect quiet just then rendered
the remark distinctly audible all over the Court.

brief description of its enemy ; —“ His missing-
link and low-grade forehead receded from two

bones rose in sallow haggardness round a nose
that stood out like Yorke’s Peninsula on the map
of Australia.”
Miss Weidermann sues the Honorable
Walpole in England for breach of promise, involv-
ing seduction. Walpole has expensive lawyers ;
Miss Weidermann has none at all, but bungles
her case and loses it. All are equal before the
law. Why cannot a Walpole face a Weidermann
before a fair judge ?
The question of a new Pope is on the
tapis. The understood principle, for long past,
has been that the Pope must be an Italian, to
obviate the jealousies of France, Germany,
Austria, Spain, and other nations, but the rule
seems quite open to abrogation. There is such a
man as Cardinal Gibbons in America.
J. G. Knight, the new Government Resi"
dent of the Northern Territory, is a regular
Poo-bah, and is regarded as an improved Czar up
that way. He has five different billets, and gets
about £6OO a year after twenty years’ service. He
is by profession an architect, and it was he who
designed Melbourne Parliament Houses.
Frank Lincoln, it appears, invested much
of his Australian-made “sugar” in “Centrals,”
just before the shares made a retrograde move-
ment. This was the only part of Lincoln’s busi-
ness which L. J. Lohr didn’t manage for him, the
latter being of opinion that operations in silver
are best confined to scooping it in at the doors.
The mother of Greville, the Court Chro-
nicler of William IV. and Victoria, lived to be
nearly 100. When Greville himself Was tolerably
ancient he “ had ” a celebrated spiritual medium
beautifully. The medium saw Greville’s dead
mother standing behind him, and so he went on
till Greville said he was just off to dine with her.
The Mining Standard appropriately gives
a notice of one of Australia’s really clever men,
Robert Wallen, of Melbourne, mining and bank-
ing expert, and journalist. His “Under the
Verandah” in one paper, and “ Talk on ’Change ’
in another, were most admirable. It was his
business journalism which established the pros-
perous and able Banking and Insurance Record.
When authors collaborate, one of them
nearly always writes all the MSS. Such is the
result of I’ecent in vestigation of the work of Scribe,
Labiche, Erckmann-Chatrian, Besant and Rice,
Sims and Pettitt, &c. This may be accounted for
by the supposition that one author is responsible
for the situations, the incidents, the naked frame-
work in short, while the other finds the language
with which these dry bones are clothed.
A purgative medicine should possess tonic and
curative, as well as cathartic, properties. This
combination of ingredients may be found in Ayer’s
Pills. They strengthen and stimulate the bowels,
causing natural action.

It is stated that the Pastoralists’ Associa-
tion will subscribe two millions with which to
fight the shearers.
The House of Lords has been struck by
lightning. The pinnacle fell and nearly killed a
judge. Augury?
Recent evidence in the Premier Building
Association case seems mighty rough upon the
Age’s “blood relation.”
Of the seven delegates chosen to represent
S.A. at the Federation Conference, jive are lawyers!
“ Beware of the scribes,” saith the Scripture.
The mutinous Grenadier Guards are to be
sent to South Africa. They should be sent to
Australia to complete their political education.
“ A straw best shows,” &c. Let us see
what motto Parkes and Co. will have embroidered
on the banners of the N.S.W. public-school cadets.
They are squabbling in the S.A. Parlia-
ment over the Silverton Tramway Agreement.

Judging from Hansard, everybody is accusing
“the other man ” of being a liar.
There is about 150 millions of English
capital invested in the Argentine Republic, where
the disturbances are. Fortunately, however, the
bondholders haven’t Egyptian influence.
Dan O’Connor, Im Pay, says he is “proud
to have the Orangemen as friends.”- He is appar-
ently much prouder of his friends the Orangemen
than he was at one awkward time of his friends
the Home Rulers.
Jimmy Munro appalled the Victorian
Assembly last week, by interjecting a pure and
beautiful truth. He said the House was degraded,
and the great voice of the country has been crying
“ Hear, hear ! ” ever since.
The N.S. Wales Minister for Works has
thoughtfully arranged that in the new and mag-
nificent Sydney Hospital a ward shall be provided
for sufferers from diseases of the jaw. Members
of Parliament will please accept this lying intima-
tion.
The Radicals, who threaten to refuse
“pairs” in future, have struck terror into the
highly-starched and very open shirt-fronts of the
dining-out, dancing eludes, chiefly Conservatives,
who look on the House of Commons as “the best
club in Europe, doncherknow.”
Desperate efforts are being made to hook
us on to Canada by cable —trying to make water
run uphill. Why all this evasion of the tele-
graphic connection between Australia and the
United States ? Because it would demoralise us,
in the opinion of the Imperialists.
Lord Rosebery is of opinion that the
cession of Heligoland to Germany will dishearten
Britons throughout the world. Not so, melud.
The man who has lived ten years in Australia as a
digger wouldn’t trouble if they swapped London
for four German sausages on a six months’ bill.
A singular letter appears in a Southern
paper about the Victorian Militia, pointing out
that they are nearly all working men, members of
trades-unions, and would side witli the prole-
tariat in a disturbance; wherefore the writer thinks
brain-workers should he in the ranks. Then
brain-workers are necessarily Tories !
John Haynes speaks of Mr Collins,
Foreign-trade candidate for the Namoi, N.S.W.,
as “a gentleman of the Jewish persuasion.”
Wrong, John, this time. A gentleman can’t be
persuaded into becoming a Jew in the same way
as he can be persuaded into becoming an Orange-
man. A Jew is a Jew “from the jump to the
judge’s box.”
Cohen, Mayor of Adelaide, went into Par
lament with a great flourish of trumpets four
year's ago. He proposed that the colony should
elect its own Governor, and do many other great
things. Strange to say, a placid calm has over-
spread Cohen since he has been elected Mayor,
and Adelaide people want to know when that re-
solution is to be tabled again.
The Victorian Premier has abandoned one
of his proposed railway lines on discovering that
it was planned to start from a howling wilderness
known as Baxter's Flat, and terminate in an un-
occupied hill. It is„alleged that the line could
have benefited nobody except the Russians, who
might choose to land in the deserted vicinity and
commence an invasion by rail.
It is of no use endeavouring to bolster up
by argument the landlord’s present legal!y-acknow-
ledged preferential claim to rent. The only class
which has the cast-iron front to support this claim
are the lawyers and those who live on precedent.
The landlord’s preference is just as unholy as was
the jus primee nocti's, and it must go the same way
as that and all the other feudal iniquities.
A number of Melbourne land-agents and
such have met to protest against the Bill for
abolishing the seignorial rights of the landlord in
the matter of distraint for rent. The alleged ob-
jection of these people lies in the statement that
any such Bill will discourage building and decrease
the employment of labour. It won’t, however.
The Bill will merely encourage land-agents to
collect rents regularly.
Mr. Fisher, N.Z. ex-Minister of Education,
now in opposition to his late colleagues, ascribes
the Budget surplus to the fact that the Govern-
ment received the succession duties on the estates
of the, late “Ready-Money” Robinson and two
other wealthy defuncts last year, George hopes
rich men will continue to become corpses so as to
ensure a surplus every year. “Now,” raid be
during a late parliamentary debate, “ Will any
rich member of the House be kind enough to die
for the good of the country v ”
There has been of late, in Sydney, a little
tin-pot agitation in the direction of having the next
N.S. W. Governor appointed from among the resi-
dents of N.S. Whiles by the Imperial authorities.
The Bulletin would as soon see the Devil ap-
pointed as Governor as any local resident who was
the choice of Downing-street. Such an appointee
would certainly be one of the old gang. The fact
of the matter is, of course, that the agitation has
been started in the supposed interests of Sir
Alfred -Stephen—aged 100 or so—whom Alfred
Bennett, Esq., of the Hevenin’ Noose, wants
appointed to govern N.S. W.
Editor Bulletin, —J. M. Macrossan, ex-
digger-man, now Colonial Secretary of Queensland,
a Roman Catholic Irishman, gave permission to
the Orangeman of Brisbane to parade the streets
with bands and regalia, and yet they never even
mentioned his name at any time during their
high festival. They cheered the Premier and the
Government, but studiously avoided the liberal
Catholic Irishman who allowed them to “ walk ”
this, mind you, after they had been refused by the
Mayor of the city, who is an Orangeman. They
evidently live on hate. —J.M., Brisbane.
Political programme of the principal N.Z.
Labour Unions :—lst. Protection to Native In-
dustries. 2nd. Progressive Land and Income Tax.
3rd. No_ future Sales of Crown Lands or Grown
Properties. 4th. Introduction of the Referendum,
so that the people may decide important measures
on which the two Houses disagree. sth. Seamen’s
Representation. (sth. Eight Hours Bill. 7th.
Increase of Parliamentary Honorarium to £2OO.
Bth. Judicious Continuation of Public Works.
9th. Special Tax on Absentee Landlords. 10th.
Uniform Set of School-books for the Colony.
The Royal Inniskilling Fusiliers, when
being conveyed by train from Portsmouth to
coerce the mutinous Guards in London, sang
“God Save Ireland.” and gave cheers for the
Guards. They were immediately replaced by a
Yorkshire regiment. A somewhat similar state of
things to that prevailing in France just prior to
the Revolution now exist in England. It may be
remembered that the National Guard, on various
occasions, sang the “Marseillaise” when they
should have shouted “ Vive le Roi!” and then,
when expected to repress disaffection, took their
places in the ranks of the disaffected. The
crumbling British throne will yet require to be
propped up by German bayonets.
Mr. Jeffreys, of the British House of
Commons, is a bounceable man with a hidden woe.
He went for the Postmaster-General recently on
the ground that members were unjustifiably re-
quired to pay postage on their correspondence,
and had even to buy their stamps personally, and
lick them, and stick them on. Consequently, he
wanted to revive the old privilege Of franking
letters, and when he was told that this couldn’t be
arranged, he wanted to know if some attendant
couldn’t moisten the stamps for them and perform
the menial job of fixing them to the envelope in
order to prevent the gum getting into the mem-
bers’ systems. But the heartless Government only
grinned a base, imbecile grin of derision and said
that gum was beneficial, and that the hon. member
should be glad to have nourishment thrown in
with his stamps.

The new Governor-elect of N.S.W., the
Earl of Jersey (who in England and Wales owns
13,1)37 acres, with a rental of £55,773), is described
as “an advanced Conservative, a genuine sports-
man, and a fluent speaker a Tory, a turf-man,
and a talker. The Bulletin has been reading the
English papers for a good many years, and blushes
to have confess that, though it believes it has
seen the Earl’s name in the racing-list (just as it
lias seen that of the Jubilee Juggins), it has failed
to store up any of the pearls of wisdom which have
fallen from his Lordship’s mouth up to date.
However, it is never too late to mend, and we are
all prepared to value the genius of his Lordship
according to certified samples thereof. However,
we hope the daily papers will not make the mis-
take of asking us to bow down to the Earl on
account of his distinguished lineage. We are not
all such benighted barbarians as to be unversed
m the doings of his sacred Majesty, George IV.
* * *
The English Governors resident in Aus-
tralia unite, as would only be natural, in sounding
the praises of the Earl of Jersey, just as the
Earl of Jersey, when he gets back to London,
will trumpet forth at the clubs the earnest
piety displayed in Sydney by Baron Car-
ington as Great Mogul of the Young Women’s
Christian Association. Of the direction in which
Lady Jersey will exercise her influence, it need
only be said that she is one of the most prominent
and loquacious orators of the Primrose League,
the apparently defunct Sydney branch of which
institution will (just you watch it!) have had a
violent revival shortly before her Ladyship reaches
the colony. Lord and Lady Jersey have five
children.
Lady Cariugton was “at home” at
Government House, Sydney, on 31st ult., and we
had fully intended to be there, but unhappily
various unforeseen circumstances intervened. The
venerable family carriage had gone to have the
hind wheel greased for one thing, and one of the
250-guinea horses had got a cold, while the other
one was employed fetching in coal. Also, the
£5OO animal with the golden shoes, which is
generally kept in reserve? had been lent to a duke,
who failed to bring it back. The family diamonds
had gone out to be newly soled and heeled, and we
were engaged in getting a new handle fixed to our
crest, and the hereditary coat-of-arms required a
patch on the hinder part of it. These opera-
tions, unfortunately, were not finished in time.
And, _ in addition to all these troubles, we had
mislaid the ancestral pedigree, and the Master of
the Horse was in a state of beastly intoxication,
and raving down in the cellar, and cursing at the
Seneschal and the Mayor of the Palace, and the
rest of the retainers ; and as if these troubles were
not sufficient, we discovered at the last moment
that somebody had left the plate and other heir-
looms out in the rain, and they had got rusted. It
was this last calamity that decided us to stay at
home and fill in the afternoon in overhauling the
title-deeds ; and consequently there was a vacancy
and a blank and a g'oom in the vice-regal halls.
# « «
A “Boys’ Purity Society”—otherwise a
Juvenile Gimlet Association—has been started in
Sydney, under the auspices of the Y.M.C.A.,
whose ruttish eyes can see the most hideous
iniquity in tights. The movement will give
us a whole host of detestable little self-righteous
hypocrites, prigs and sneaks. The Pure Boy
must never, at risk of breaking his oath, use a
swear-word, and will, when he attains perfection,
be like the sanctified engineer on the New Jersey
Central Railroad who never cussed in his life, no
matter what happened. In an accident his loco-
motive was smashed all to pieces. The engineer
walked around the wreck, gazed at it mournfully,
paused, and ejaculated: “Oh, fiddle!”
» * *
Mr. Wimble, of Queensland, finds divorce
an expensive game. His wife, having got a decree
against him, has applied for alimony, and lias been
granted £l5O per annum for life—that is to say, so
long as he and she do live or (she only surviving)
his estate is good for the money.
* * #
Mr. Bertrand, dentist and murderer, used
to drug his lady patients before submitting them
to nameless indignities. Mr. Bertrand set down
his exploits in his diary, and as in it the names of
several Sydney ladies of high social position were
compromised, he will never get out of gaol, as does
the ordinary reprieved murderer who serves his
twenty years or so—as a matter of fact he has now
been in prison for nearly a quarter of a century.
He is the victim not so much of the Law as of" a
personal revenge to which effect cannot be given
in ordinary cases.
’* * #
Now there are extensively - circulated
rumours of an assault said to have been committed
by a Sydney dentist on a young lady under the
influence of an anaesthetic. In justice to the
dentist and his profession the statements should
be closely investigated by an astute detective.
* * #
A Melbourne journalist was sent to “do ”
& Mothers’ Meeting in Collingwood, a gathering of
the rich and quasi-scientific tabbies, to preach at
the poor and ignorant. It happened that a kid-
gloved predecessor had been soaping down the
tabs with “ A great work being done ” and similar
tommy-rot, but the new man wrote that it was all
humbug—half a dozen common women lectured
by a dozen stiff-’uns. He said they went the
wrong way to work and ought to come down to
the common women’s level, really associate with
them, and so on. The honorary lady secretary
replied tartly in a letter to the paper: “Your
reporter is quite wrong. For the time being we
do associate with these people.” The journalist
returned to the charge with “ Look here, that’s
just what the common people resent, your asso-
ciation ‘ for the time being. ’ ”
# * #
Melbourne residents are surprised to see
such a venerable business as Henty and Co. ’s to
let. It brings to mind how Mortimer Franklyn’s
papers mopped up £90,000, the accumulation of
the old-time Henty toil. One plants and another
gathers, with newspaper plants. Everybody is
sorry for the Hentys, who were real aristocrats
from a human point of view—for they were an
honourable and generous family—men, women,
and children. As a rule, there is little sympathy
among the masses for fallen wealth, but all Aus-
tralia is genuinely sorry for the misfortunes of
Victoria’s old historic family.
# * *
The ladies who come into town laden with
fiddle-cases are not going to a music-master’s —-
any more than the swells who do the Block carry-
ing tennis-bats to keep up their social positions
are going to play lawn-tennis. Those ladies are
shopping. On returning, the fiddle-case may con-
tain remnants, ribbons, feathers, &c., or it may
contain tea, sugar, candles and bacon—each item
bought at some grocery that makes a specialty of
the cheap sale of one particular commodity. We
have watched them at it.

should be bound up with Ovid’s Art of Love—
then people would have both diagnosis and cure-
poison and antidote.

Our recent visitor, ex-contractor Half-
crown Brassey, says the cable, is about to tie his
second nuptial knot with the widow of the eldest
son of the Earl of Essex, a nobleman who, by the
way, is not connected with “good’’Queen Bess’s
mash of the same name. The Bulletin can see
no just cause or impediment why a rich, common-
place person should not hitch himself to a widow
named De Vere, if they both feel so disposed, but
as an item of Australasian interest, the contem-
plated union is of no more importance than a
dead donkey.

a Northern scribe, “are not so bad, and yet so far

Brisbane than a reporter.”
* * #
The proprietors of a totalisator, at Adelaide,
sent a contribution of £B, being part of the profits
of their uncircumcised implement, to the trustees
of the Female Refuge, the other day; but the
pious b ard struck an attitude like Ajax defying
the lightning when he felt sure it was going to hit
somebody else, and handed the coin back. They
also mentioned something about the wages of sin
being death, and made a side allusion to an animal
mentioned in Scripture which is understood to be
unclean and not a fit specimen to be allowed to
sleep under the bed. The totalisator people now
point out indignantly that the board didn’t sub-
scribe £8 of tueir own money to make up the loss
which their outraged feelings caused to the unfor-
tunate inmates of the Refuge, and hint that it is a
very inexpensive thing to sacrifice. £8 which was
meant for the poor, and to strike imposing atti-
tudes when some one else has to pay for them.
They propose, therefore, that the board should
defray the cost of its own lofty indignation in
future, but that body declines to hold any further
communication with the children of sin, and the
subject has dropped.

Melbourne Savings Bank opens a new
branch at once on the road to Flemington race-
course. Abrahams, Isaacs and Jacobs are
expected to figure largely among depositors.
* * *
Through one of those vicissitudes inci-
dental to “colonial experience ” a certain reporter
was once employed in cutting burrs on the Mur-
rumbidgee. One morning news arrived at the
burr-camp that a volunteer was wanted at the
home station to replace the bullock-driver, who
had left without notice. The boss told the re-
porter to try the job as “you’re no dashed good
here.” The team set out on a week's journey, and
all went well for the first day. The express-man
spent the night comfortably in a hammock slung
bullocky fashion beneath the dray-pole, and next
morning succeeded in yoking-up the brutes—the
most trying part of the profession. About mid-
day the bullocks, crossing some soft ground, got
“ stuck,” and refused to move. The reporter tried
the usual remedy, hard swearing, but in vain.
Hi s vocabulary of oaths having utterly failed, he
loudly addressed the team in such Parisian
phrases as “ Have you seen the dog of my uncle?”
“Give me the knife of your father.” No good.
Then a brilliant idea struck him. He imagined
himself Editor Winter in Melbourne Herald
composing-room with the “setting” ten minutes
behind. Ere the day was over the team had made
a good record.

* * #
A London policeman ran a woman in the
other week, because she “looked as if she was
wishing people to give her money.” The magis-
trate, Montagu Williams, suggested that quite a
host of people were always wishing the same thing,
and promptly discharged the poor creature without
the option of three months’ imprisonment. Then
it dawned upon Constable X. that he himself
usually looked as if ho wished people to give him
money.

of him. She did. She is still doing so.

The fact that the Australian Cities Invest-
ment Corporation, formed in London with a capi-
tal of half-a-million sterling, are first going to
operate on the “ Kensington ” estate, near Sydney
reminds us that the old “Scotchman” claim, at
Charters Towers, was the first Australian mine
sold by Pritchard Morgan in London. “ But
Scotchman ’ won’t do,” said the astute Pritchard
-—“let’s call it ‘ the Disraeli.’ The English hate
the Scotch—havq hated ’em ever since and before
the time of Doctor Johnson.” And Disraeli it
was.

Lord Duulo, the titled idiot who has been
egged on by his relatives and “ friends ”to divorce
the music-hall singer whom he married and then
left at the church-door at the instigation of the
relatives and friends aforesaid, seems, at time of
writing, quite unlikely to succeed in legally
getting rid of the lady, who Wanted to accompany
him to Australia, but was prevented from doing so
by the nobility, and was left to support herself
in her own way during the indefinite absence of
her distinguished husband, who now says he
“tossed up for her” with two other men'of his
own kidney. In point of brains she is far too
good for such a hopeless numskull, and so far as
what is known as “morality” is concerned, she
can't be much worse than Dunlo’s father, the Earl
of Clancarty, who admits, with a calm im-
pudence born of hereditary privilege, that ho
gave Lewis and Lewis, the “smarty” London
solicitors, “carte blanche to get rid of” the
1 ileasant-looking, little music-hall dancer. If
Dunlo is a fair sample of the Clancarty family,
then it is quite certain that it will not be safe to
let any of them off the chain at full-moon until
the head of the house shall have eloped with an
athletic music-hall dancer or a healthy cook,
and produced an intelligent heir to the title.

A pair who were divorced in America
seventeen years ago met and re-married in Sydney
not long since. And, by the way, a couple whose
quarrels ended in a divorce, the details of which
will long be remembered in Sydney Hebraic
circles, have re-married and are now living har-
moniously together.
* * *
In the interest of the common people,
Melbourne Hospital must be pulled down. In
the interest of the common people Sydney Hos-
pital must be built up. The strong opposition in
certain influential quarters to the building of a
large and permanent hospital in Macquarie-street
is mainly due to the fact that it is considered un
desirable to locate hospital patients on so valuable
and central a site in so fashionable a residential
quarter, while cheaper accessible land can so easily
be found. But no site can be too good for a hos-
pital, and that decided on is one of the most
glorious in the world. It surpasses in the beauty
of its outlook any with which we are acquainted.
* * #
Capital punishment evidently is about to
be abolished in the United States, remarks a Lon-
don paper, and the abolition is coming about in a
somewhat strange way. No doubt there is a
growing public feeling against it. But in the
State of New York this feeling is supported by
the electric companies. Why? Because, the law
having already substituted electricity for the
hangman’s rope, the electric companies are afraid
that the association of “ current ” with death will
tell against their business.

at Sydney.” “The genial Club.” That is how
C. A. S. disposes of the Yoriek, the Melbourne
literary club for whose members he has professed
so profound a contempt.

“O Noble Workers!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ii Noble workers we have built up a great empire on
vhioh the sun never sets—tho wealthiest nation in the
vorkh Here (distributing money) is your share, three-
•iml-si'xpence a day, and (smiling benignly) here is an
‘ tra „ voat each, go and drink my health (1, Plugson, the
Capitalist.) O noble heroic freemen.”— Carlyle's Ghost.
you’re in the land of bondage yet,
Though sophists call you “free
Lo ! when thy masters pass ye by
In thought you bend the knee.
Your children bred as patient thralls
By cruel laws kept down —
Thy daughters sweep the rich man’s halls,
Or stitch his lady’s gown.
Ye drudge and moil thy whole life through
To keep the wolf at bay,
And ’fore thy offspring’s bones are set
They sweat for paltry pay.
The Rich, they go in garments fine,
In Pleasure’s path to roam,
And with the gold your sinews earn
They build each marble home.
They’ve leisure and they’ve luxury,
' And plenty for their share,
And silken robes resplendent bright
Bedeck their ladies fair.
While yours—they drudge in sordid homes,
Although ye are the strong;
And so you’re tamely shorn like sheep—
How long, O Lord, how long ?
New Zealand.
ARTHUR DESMOND.
Migraine.
Nervous headaches and neuralgia are the first,
surest, and most reliable symptoms of approaching
decline of physical health. They are distinctly
nerve pains, an infallible telegraph message prog-
nosticative of approaching physical weakness.
They are intimations that the strength is being
impaired. Overwork of any description must
result in enervation and enfeeblement of the
corporeal structure. This wear and tear (termed
by scientists destructive metabolism) may be
brought about by worry, excessive mental or
physical labor, by continual attention to business,
by bad food, bad air or any acute sickness, the
duties of maternity, the strain of motherhood,
dissipation, and many other causes. The proper
remedy is Clements Tonic. This must and will
cure the worst and most severe cases. This is a
positive fact; it has done it, and will do it again
always. We are thus emphatic because we know
we are talking facts, which the evidence of dis-
interested persons proves. Mr. Henry A. Crane,
a gentleman well known all over the colony writes :
Dear Sir, —For the last seven years I have been a
victim to the most excruciating agony from
neuralgia, and also suffered from extreme nervous-
ness. I tried hundreds of so-called specifics,
but without avail; and, being a commercial
traveller, am particularly liable to colds frtm
exposure to draughts, &o. ; a slight one even
always fled to my facial nerves, and caused
neuralgic pains, preventing sleep and causing
excessive sympathetic headache. I saw Clements
Tonic advertised, procured a bottle, and in two
days the pain was completely cured, a c nsumma-
tion I had never expected, and am deeply thankful
for same. This was four months ago, and from
then till now I have never had the slightest twinge
of the-excruciating torture I used to have. May
make whatever use of this you like. W. H. East,
Esq., Stanthorpe, Dulwich Hill, N.S.W., suffered
a martyrdom of headaches, due to disordered liver
and indigestion, frequently having to leave his
work in the middle of the day. He took a
course of Clements Tonic, and writes: Stan-
thorpe, Dulwich-street, Petersham.—Dear Sir,
—For years I have been a great sufferer
from headache, which has at times so pro-
strated me that I have been totally unable
to attend to my work. I have consulted the
most skilled physicians in Sydney, and spent con-
siderable money in fees and medicine. I, how-
ever, obtained only temporary relief, as they
treated the effect, and not the cause. I was re-
commended Clements Tonic, and determined to
test its efficacy, and I am very grateful for the
good results which have followed its use. I took
five bottles, and am wonderfully improved, and
have had no return of those excruciating head-
aches since taking that valuable remedy. Results
like this must encourage your laudable efforts
to alleviate the sufferings of your fellow-
men ; and you have my sanction to publish
this. Sydney Cunningham, Esq., 70, Watkin-
street, Newtown (N.S.W.), writes as follows :
Dear Sir, —Having been a long sufferer from
neuralgia, which weakened me considerably, after
using various remedies without benefit, I was
induced to give Clements Tonic a trial, find am
pleased to inform you that the result exceeded my
utmost expectations, and after taking five large
bottles I feel better than ever I did in my life
before. My wife and other members of our family
have used it with equal success, and I can strongly
recommend it to others suffering in a like manner,
and offer you this unsolicited testimonial to use as
you think fit. Reader, can any other medicine
show such a record of cures as Clements Tonic ?
We think not.

The Melbourne Freetrade Democrats are
a queer lot, and one told a meeting the other night
that £1 would go further in (Protectionist!) New
Zealand than £2 or maybe £3 in Australia, where-
fore New Zealanders were ever so much happier
on lower wages. Now this is gross as a mountain,
open, palpable. This ex-New Zealander must be a
Yankee liar working West.

Parliament —Partial Men.
Charles Gavan Duffy, aged seventy-some-
thing, is about to wed a lady of seven-and-twenty.
That will make four. It reminds us of the old
Sydney magnate who has had six or seven spouses
and who, whenever he has just married a new one,
takes her to his wife-plantation and says : “ Look,
Mary Jane lies here —and that is where poor
Agnes is buried ” —and so on.
A Melbourne paper reminds ns that De
Freitas, the Christian Portuguese, owner of the
big Mechanics’ Restaurant, Bourke-street, still
holds his “eight o’clock rush.” For many years
this philanthropist has laid about 70 free covers
nightly, and of such is the Kingdom of Heaven —
Freitas and the dossing Lazaruses. Freitas is a
Royalist, and hangs up portraits of the Emperor
of Brazil.
Louise Michel began life as a governess in
the family of the Count de Soissons, and her first
exploit was to smash the crucifixes belonging, to
her pupils, the Count’s daughters, and to dance
round the Count’s dining-table denouncing the
parish priest, who was sitting at meat with the
family. Louise has for years devoted all the
money earned by lectures and press-work to the
support of her aged mother and to charitable and
revolutionary purposes.

ha n and the CoUntess of Jersey, Mrs. Fitzherbert,
and.Perdita Robinson. George IY. was up to the
list furnished by Lepdrello to Don Giovanni.
However, Wallace was a good sort who came in for
tho Hertford cash and disbursed it generously.
But the Hertford line is extinct.

A governor of New Caledonia assumed
his authority while the natives were still can-
nibals. There had been rumours of an insurrec-
tion. and the admiral called before him a native
chief, who was faithful to the French cause, and
questioned him as to their truth. “You may be
sure,” said the native, “that there will be no war
at present, because the yams are yet far from being
ripe.” “ The yams, you say ?” “Yes. Our people
never make war except when the yams are ripe. ”
“Why is that?” “Because baked yams go so
very well with the captives,”
Amelie Rives,, the hysterical authoress of
the “Quick and the Dead,’’ has a rival, Nellie
Littell, who has wrested from her the bun of
notoriety lay appearing in Central Park (N. Y.)
astride of a horse. She is described as a “ well-
built, ruddy-cheeked damsel, with a touch of red
in her nut-brown hair, and a laughing, but deter-
mined, glance in her large grey eyes. She rode a
spirited mustang, and was dressed in a broad-
brimmed hat with a black ostrich-feather, a cut-
away jacket, and a bifurcated cloth skirt, beneath
which were knickerbockers of a similar material,
and high boots.”
| ‘When Anthony Trollope edited St. Paul’s
Magazine— both editor and magazine are now out
of publication—he interviewed a well-known lady
novelist, in order to obtain a novel from her pen
which would run through the magazine as a serial.
The lady refused to allow any of her works to be
thus mutilated. “ But,” said Trollope. “it is
necessary for the purposes of a magazine, and
Thackeray and Dickens have done it.” This
roused the lady’s ire. “Would you compare my
writings,” said she, “with the things of Thackeray
and Dickens?” “Can’t,” said Trollope, in his
gruffest manner; “never read a word of your
writings.”
Philippa Fawcett, who beat the senior
wrangler at Cambridge, is a daughter of the late
Professor Henry Fawcett, Gladstone’s blind Post-
master-General, and his wife Milicent, sister of
Mrs. Garrett Anderson, the well-known lady-
doctor, who visited Australia a few years ago, and
is the wife of the managing director of the Orient
Steam Navigation Co. Singularly enough, one of
the senior wranglers defeated by Miss Fawcett is
also blind. The lady usually wears gowns of her
own manufacture. Newnham College turned out
another Cambridge lady wrangler some years ago,
Miss Blanche Hewett, now head-mistress of a
girls’ school.
In “ The Life and Correspondence of the
late Dr. Doyle ” (R.C. Bishop) occurs the follow-
ing story Examining the students at Carlow
College, the Doctor said to one of the students
named Garrett: “Supposing you were saying
Mass, and let a spider fall into the chalice, would
you feel yourself authorised in picking it out G
Garrett was silent, but, having been prompted by
another student, he replied : “ I would swallow it,
my Lord.” “ Very good,” said Dr. Doyle ; “ and
if a donkey fell into the chalice, what course would
yon adopt?” “Swallow it,” responded Garrett,
mechanically. “Hoofs and all?” inquired the
Bishop. “Yes, ’replied Garrett, to the infinite
amusement of the assemblage.
A French book, just translated into
English, has gained an enormous circulation, all
over Europe. It is the- Autobiography of Marie
Bashkirtseff, an eminent Russian painter, who
died six years ago, aged only 24. The diary was
kept from the age of 12 up to her death, record-
ing minutely a 1 her feelings, on the basis of
Rousseau’s Confessions. She.and Bastien Lepage,
the immortal landscape-painter, were in love with
each other, and. both dying. Their couches were
in one room, and it was a race which should die
first, the winner only gaining by a few weeks. Of
the book its translator, Mathilde Blind, says :
“ To read it is an education in psychology.”
Lydia Becker, an old-time champion of
Woman’s Rights, died the other Saturday.
Twenty years ago Lydia was a laughing-stock to
the ponderous “ representative ” persons, and
mechanical pressmen, who mocked at lady doctors,
lady students, and lady thinkers of all grades and
conditions. Lydia was in truth a somewhat mas-
culine virgin, as were most of the early sisters of
the “movement,” but so far from being the eccen-
tric individual supposed in the dark ages of 18G0-70
she never advocated any Light which is not freely
recognised by intelligent men to-day. Lydia died
happy, no doubt, in the knowledge that her life-
work was, to all real intents and purposes, accom-
plished.
The most successful of the ex-parsons in
the Victorian Legislature has been Davies, the
Minister of Mines—nothing in blood to the gilded
and titled Speaker, or in money either. He
swarmed into Parliament about 20 years ago, with
a terrific batch of ex clergymen, so that there was
an outcry about the pulpit being made a stepping-
stone to politics, under the wing of loyal liberalism,
M'Cullochi m and Higinbothamy; but Davies
never was a proper parson, his education having
been scientific, which makes him a fit Mining
Minister. The scroll of parliamentary preachers
has been endless : Cooper, Bell, Richardson,
James, and.so on. To-day the pulpit’s played out.
David Blair was the first conspicuous parson in
the Southern Parliament.
Tough yarn from London paper ;
The recent death of David Buchanan, apr ominent poli-
tician of N.S.W., lias brought to light a characteristic
anecdote of Carlyle. Many years ago Sir Henry Barkes,
the present Premier of that colony, came to England as
an emigration lecturer, and made the acquaintance of
several distinguished men. Amongst others upon whom
he called was the grim Sage of Chelsea, with whom he
had a lengthened conversation, and he went away laying
the flattering unction to his soul that he had made a
distinctly favour'’hie impression upon Carlyle. Some,
time afterwards Mr. Buchanan left Sydney for (ondon to
study law in the Middle Temple, and he was commis-
sioned by Sir Henry Parkes to deliver a fine opossum rug
as a present to Carlyle from his antipodean friend and
admirer. Buchanan, soon after his arrival in London,
went down to Chelsea, was ushered into the Sage’s pre-
sence, and said ho had been charged by Sir Henry Parkes
to deliver “ this handsome present.” Then there was an
awkward pause ; the Sage sat silently thinking, and at
last he growled out, “I remember him-a babbling
gorilla.”

Collins v. Murphy, Melbourne Court,
ownership of magpie. Collins family lost Maggie;
found one like it in Murphy phnmilee; identity
denied. Test point: Collins’ magpie used to say
“ Milk, oil,” when the milkman came, but all the
tickling under its stomach wouldn’t make it say
“Milk, oh,” in court. Just as the Murphys were
carrying it off in triumph it yelled “Milk, oh.”
Verdict reversed.
A whole host of Bulletin paragraphs and
two long Bulletin articles, after travelling round
the world, have during the last week, found their
way back to Sydney and appeared in the columns
of the local dailies. This city’s papers only appre-
ciate Bulletin matter after it has received the
hall-mark of British approval. In England and
America, The Bulletin is immeasurably the best-
quoted Australian paper. By the way, “Scotty
the Wrinkler’s” chaining little tale “ Only For
Company,” which appeared in this journal a few
months ago, is quoted in several influential British
and American papers as a model st ry—which
quite coincides with- our own idea that, though
an unpretentious trifle, no cleaner-cut or more
artistic piece of literary work has been done in
Australia for t ■wen ty years.
The fury of lovely woman’s wrath has
descended, by an unbeaten track, upon John
McDonald, a gentleman engaged in digging last-
resting-places at Melbourne General Cemetery.
The other morning John was following his avoca-
tion six feet below the surface of the old-
established bone-yard in question, when the calm
serenity of his daily toil was disturbed by a
shower of bricks, and before he had time to enquire
within as to whether it was Resurrection Day
and the adjacent corpses were stopping to give
him one for his nob on their way to Judgment,
the awful truth of the matter glared down upon
John from the brink. Mrs. Walsh had arrived,
and was slinging the large, oblong pieces of baked
clay with her own fair hands. John, away down
at the bottom of the excavation, knew well enough
that this indomitable female had tracked him
to his lair at last. He didn’t ask for
an explanation of her conduct, because his
conscience told him that she was obtaining repara-
tion of his sins in luring old man Walsh to the
pub. and keeping that enviable husband and father
away from his happy home. In any case, more-
over, John couldn’t open his mouth to expostulate
with the bricks which were fast laying him low for
ever. Every time he emerged from the debris and
attempted to escape from his place of immurement
the bruised social misdemeanant found himself
shoved back upon a coffin-foundation by Mrs.
Walsh’s umbrella, and the work of burying John
beneath the ruins progressed steadily. At last an
emissary from Providence, in the shape of a
brother grave-digger, was attracted to the spot-by
John’s awful groans sand u iched between the sicken-
ing thuds o, the - avenging missiles, and finally
this reinforcement succeeded in beating back the
enemy with a pick-axe, while he rescued Mr.
M‘Donald from his dreadful position in the bowels
of the earth. At this stage, too, the police
arrived, and carried away Mrs. Walsh to liquidate
her liabilities with 205., or, in default, seven days
imprisonment. She’s all right, so to speak, but
John M‘Donald now makes his daily descents
into the cold ground with the heavy heart of a
man who knows not at what moment the blow may
f all and the brick be visited upon him. Between
each prod of the spade he glances upwards, ex-
pecting to see Walsh’s missis in the act of cutting
him down like a flower.


Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors.
Strictly Original matter contributed to Tub Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under ; —
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-hoard.)
5. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
h. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
6, Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. The
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on thcMS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

The present British hangman’s autobio-
graphy begins its existence in print with an
edition of 50,000 copies. One chapter is horribly
entitled, “Men and Women I have Executed.”
Another financial crash in Melbourne,
with the old moral of the audit farce. Five
months ago there was a roseate statement and a
ten per cent, dividend.
Zox’s latest rival in the feebly punning
line is Hopetoun. At Melbourne Aquarium t:e
other day, the latter said that the big seal had made
a great impression upon him. (Great laughter).
Not an island has risen or sunk from sight
in the Pacific Ocean for 34 years, and geologists
say that nature is resting for a future mighty
effort. An English geologist predicts that within
50 years a convulsion of native will sink the whole
of New Zealand 50ft. below the surface of the sea.
Wolfe’s Schnapps—popularity increases yearly.
In the Moi’daunt case the Prince of Wales
was not examined “on oath” at all, nor was it
before the House of Lords. He was called as a
witness in the divorce-court, Lord Penzance pre-
siding. The court declined to have him sworn,
stating that they were prepared to accept “his
word of honour.”
A Bill relating to the law of evidence,
now before the Victorian Legislature, provides
that no barrister shall “ insult or annoy a witness.”
Considering that that is about the only thing that
a barrister is good for this seems a most instilling
proposal. The very first duty of the “ learned
counsel ” is to rush dojvn on the testifier in the
box like the wolf coming down on the fold, and
reriiind him that “You are on your oath, sir,” tell
him that he ought to be in the dock, and ask him
if he “ would be surprised to hear ” something that
never took place.
Rev. Shirley Baker must find it hard to
maintain a meek and humble deportment at the
present time. Only a short while ago he was
Pope ot Tonga. He was 'also Premier, and prac-
tically King. Now he is none of these, and every-

“I received the watch safe sent to be repaired,
and it is now going as well as ever. I have had it
owo years, and it always kept first-class time, tdl
I overwound it. I have carried a watch for forty
years, and never found one to give me the satisfac-
tion of the Waterbury, although 1 have paid
twenty guineas for one.”
Mr. Arthur Taplin, Cobbity, writes: *’l’m
peifectly satisfied with the Waterbury watch I
have in my possession. I see a good many of the
I watches, and it is the general opinion that they are
j good, cheap watches.”
From Cowra, “F.A.” writes: “My watch
I came safely to hand. It has given me great satis-
| faction since I bought it about two years ago.
Although it has had very rough usage, it has
! proved a good timekeeper, and I feel .confident,
that now, after cleaning, it will be as good a 8
ever. ”
