The Referee.
Bookmaker Alf. Joseph must be posing in Eng-
land as a kind of leading Australian institution, whereby
British opinion of these beastly kawlinies is likely to lie
Jiuch chastened and improved, The Bulletin imaji'nes.
a reporter from the London Si-ortsman waited upon
Alfred the other day, and asked that swollen, suetty-
lookin 0- person if he didn’t hold, “many public positions”
(independent of his glittering social position) in his own
country. To which Alfred replied with a puffy affirma-
tive and was good enough to furnish a few details of his
o-reatness. “At present lam president of the Sandhurst
Racing Club, vice-president of the cricket club (one
“over” with the ball or two pulls at a pair of sculls would
cause Alfred to die in great agony), and I hold
ggwral other public positions,” says the distinguished
visitor from Australia. Evidently there was something in
aI-L’s lofty mariner of reply which suggested Cabinet,
secrets and the Federation Conference, because the
reporter visibly trembled and explained—" I wilt not ask
you anything political, neither will I question you about
the wonderful resources of your country’’—just as
though the fat hero understood any resources except
laying the odds. The reporter restricted himself to
racing questions, all of which the “leviathan”, answered
in a spirit of simple truth, claiming vast, superiority for
Australian management, and admitting the sit; e.iority ox
British jockeyship and training. Itis baldly necessary to
republish here the opinion of Alf. Joseph on matters
concerning which some thousand or two new-chum '
possessing a thorough knowledge of racing in
E no-land could speak with more authority. Th.it the
arrangements at Flemington, Randwick and Caulfield put
Newmarket, Ascot, &c., to the blush is as certain as that '
the finish of a big English race is a work of art fa t
beyond the comprehension of our Australian jocks
Australian bushmen arc masters in horsemanship,
pure and simple, just as Texas “cowboys” are,
but for men who thoroughly understand how to
nurse a thoroughbred in a race across the fiat we'must
still go to the country where such racing has been longest
established. The Americans discovered, years ago, that
thbir own riders were not good enough to put up in
England, where some of the best jockeys are primed w ith
the° accumulated lore of a line of ancestors, all of whom
wore silk in their respective days. It is not surprising
that such hereditary artists “ amazed,” actually amazed, a
man of Alfred’s rank and station by their skill and
cunning.
* * *
Judging from Sydney Metropolitan entries, the
A.J.C. Will have to make some drastic alteration in the
conditions of the race if they have any idea of keeping
pace with their great Southern rival. Take Sir William,
Marvel, Antseus, The Queen and Prince Consort from the
list, and on performances there is not a first-class animal
left. This is easily accounted for. The Metropolitan of
£lOOO is run before the Melbourne Cup of £lO,OOO, and as
the winner of the former incurs a penalty of 1411)., itis
not likely an owner, who considers lie has a show for the
Cup, wilt run the risk of having a stone put upon his
horse’s back for the big event. Again, the weights fox
the Melbourne Cup were published some time since, whilst
the imposts for the minor race are still unborn. To make
the Sydney Metropolitan worthy the wealthy club under
whose auspices it is run, there are two courses open to the
Australian Jockey Club. Make the. race worth winning
in these days of big stakes, or run it off after the Mel-
bourne Cup. This question cannot be longer shirked.
* # *
Melbourne Standard has hired a poet to fur-
nish horse information inverse. This person was. called
upon in the course of business to advise upon the Grand
National Steeplechase, so he shut himself up with a pint
of ink and an inspiration, and the spirit moved him. Pos-
sibly he was rather heavy to lift, however, for the spirit
didn’t move him far, arid it seems to have dropped him
again pretty soon. Here is a sample :
On the first day of the meet the tipsters were out,
And the question is asked, “ What were they about?”
They knew the uncertainty there was with Bravo
With old Ellerslie in they should have said, “ Why—iust
so 1” . . .
So the poet speaks out and will let you all in
To the secret of luck, and you all ought to win ;
Of the foremost flight, when the straight is reached,
The names of “Lawler,” “Sir Joshua,” and “Titoka
warn ” ’ll be screeched.
The remainder of the poem is too painful for reproduc-
tion. The inspiration gave out at this point, and the
metre broke down, and the rhyme died a natural death ;
and the poet was left stranded high up on a top note
amid a chaos of exploded grammar and decomposed pro-
nunciation. But he held on manfully til! the last, and
crowded in every horse he wanted to mention, and if the
horse was too big to go in comfortably, lie was shoved in
by main force. He said what he wanted to say whether
the verse was large enough to hold'it or not. Tho first
business of a poet is to unfold a plain, unvarnished tale,
and the Staniurd’.s bard evidently realises this. When
the tale won’t unfold, its owner had better coil it up and
sit down upon it , and keep quiet.

Billy Murphy duly returned to Sydney per
Alameda, and his inarch dow n the gangway fairly fetched
the unwashed waiting for him on Woolloomooloo wharf.
William was attired in a very “ pee-wee ” hat and a long
ulster, carried in his smashing right hand three presenta-
tion walking-sticks, arid was followed by a faithful hench-
man, who humped a box containing the 660-guinea cham-
pion bolt, now on view in King-street. Murphy will
shortly meet “Griffo” at the Sydney Young Men’s
Chris— ~we mean Amateur Gymnastic Club, for a purse
“to a finish,” but the, writer does not think Murphy will
part with his mortgage on the belt on that occasion.
Murphy is said to have cleared £2OOO in the States. At
Honolulu the King gave him a walking-stick and a purse
of sovereigns.

The death of Mcßride, an aspirant to pugilistic
rewards whose organs were diseased, having occurred at
San Francisco at a time when slogging enthusiasm was on
the wane and officialdom was agitated by one of its
periodical spasms of energetic morality, lias been made an
excuse to put down prize-fighting in that city with the
strong arm of the policeman. That is the reason why
Murphy lias returned to Sydney, why Peter Jackson's
return is expected, and for McCarthy’s flight to England.
Now, what is to become of Fitzsimmons, Mnlholland, and
other Australians who are out in the Californian cold?
The poor devils will actually have to go to work.

The English cricket-going public must, dote on
Barrett, who was at the -wicket for a solid hour, in the
first international match, without scoring a run. A few
more batsmen like Barrett and the national game would
scon die from unnatural causes, unless saved by a rule

precious “patience,” is coming out as a comparative
slogger this season.
» #
Everyman has a natural style of “ shaping,”
and if the first time he “puts up his hands,” the right hand
and foot come to tire front, that is the style in which
Nature has intended him to “put a heart ”• -on.his. oppo-
nent. Should he take lessons from a professor ”of , the
“ noble art,” the professor will at once insist upon his
changing his style, simply because the right hand in front
will upset ail the stereotyped hit- stop-joVeand-get-away
business, and the teacher is at a loss how to instruct his
pupil. The right-hand-in-front man lias all the best of
the bargain, as he is probably the first man who has stood
that way in front of his opponent, who cannot make head
or tail of a man who does not stand in the orthodox
manner, whilst the right-hander is quite at home, having
been accustomed to box thos ■ who keep the left in front.
The two best amateurs the writer has seen in Sydney were
both right in front, Mr. George Seale and Mr. “Jack”
•Tones, the latter, by the way, an uncle to Mr. “ Sammy ”
•Tones, the cricketer. When George Seale first went to
Larry Foley to take lessons the latter tried hard to in-
duce him to change hands, but without success. Had he
been successful the writer really believes the phenomenal
amateur would never have been heard of.

land, and in one or two cases under the 10.

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, _of 4
.Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank lias
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with.

Wolfe’s Schnapps, the purest spirit procurable.
'As nearly all the world knows, Stanley is
blessed with a head of white hair. His own
reason for the premature paleness of his tonsorial
thatch is the suffering and hardship he endured in
the great forest, but a different version has reached
our ears. In the last expedition on .one occasion
Stanley asked his officers to travel as lightly as
they possibly could, which request was complied
with, Major Bartlett even leaving behind his camp
bedstead, an almost absolute necessity when sleep-
ing out in the forests. On arrival at the station
this officer, on going over the baggage, found
amongst that of <he great pioneer a case, not of
brandy, but of Mrs. Allen’s hair restorer and
dye. Enraged at finding that he had sacrificed
his comfort for Stanley’s vanity, Major B. ordered
the bottles to be left behind, and the African
Pathfinder’s cranium gradually lost its erewhile
chestnut bloom, and. slowly assumed its normal
greyness. Hen e his present venerable and patri-
archal appearance.—Ex.

HUM OUR OF A CRANK.
HOW A PREACHER TALKED IN RHYME AND HOW
TO CURE HIS WANDERING FANCY.
In tho west of the United .States a clergyman
was seized with a rhyming mania. Every request
he made was in impromptu rhyme, and lie began
to deliver his sermons in high-flown doggerel. His
name, was supposed to be Snyder. Except for this
ho was perfectly sane. His friends advised him to
lay his case before the celebrated Dr. Hammond,
of New York, which lie did, journeying thither.
His first words were, on entering the doctor’s
office, as follows
“Good morning, Dr. Hammond, I hope that you
are well;
I’ve come a long distance my story to tell.
They say I'm insane, but that’s inanity ;
I've a rhyming inspiration, but that’s not in-
sanity.”
Whether Dr. Hammond cured his patient or not
is not stated, but the case is susceptible of easy
treatment, as it is only a mild lunacy. It would
cure itself if the subject was fortunately or unfor-
tunately stricken with pain of long continuance.
Pain would serve as a counter irritant, and it
knocks the poetry out of anything. Suddenly
attacked with the pangs of rheumatism, the
twitching aches of neuralgia, the acute tortures of
sciatica and lumbago, or the dull misery of sprain o ,
hurts, or bruises, which acting as a diversion to
such a perverted brain, the patient would talk no
more rhyme. As the folly wore off it would Ire
easy enough to cure the pain in any form, promptly
and permanently, in the manner followed by those
who know what is the best for it. Mr. W. Booth,
Thomas-street, Balmain, states : “ Had severe
rheumatism and no end of pain. After one or
two applications of St. Jacobs oil pain entirely
left me. Recommended it to my friends. My
wife was cured of neuralgia in the same way.”
“A member of my family,” says Mr. J. R.
Drewett, Glen View, Darlinghurst, “ was cured
by. St. Jacobs oil of the excruciating tortures
of rheumatic gout.” Mr. James O’Brien, stock
broker, Sandhurst (Victoria) says ; “ With less
than half a bottle of St. Jacobs oil I was cured of
acute rheumatism.” Mr. Arthur Pretty, 5 Col-
lins-street, Melbourne, states “that a bottle of
St. Jacobs oil cured him of chronic rheumatism.
He had no faith in it, but was intensely surprised
and gratified.” Mr. E. A. Little,,2ol Pitt-street,
Sydney, says he “is glad to testify that his wife
was cured of a painful rheumatic affection by St.
Jacobs oil.” Air. R. C. Payne, Brisbane) say’s :
“Ihave received instantaneous relief from acute
rheumatism of long standing by the use of St.
Jacobs oil.” Mr. Allister Brown, Auckland,
N.Z., writes: “Half a bottle of St. Jacobs oil
cured me of rheumatism so severe. I could liot lift
my arm.”, In like manner Air. D. Pass (of Feld-
hej-ni, Gotthelf, and Co,), Sydney; Mr. John D.
Flower, Balmain; Win. Lovering (S. Hoffnung
and Co.), Sydney; T. W. Allen (A. H. Nathan
and Co.), Auckland ; Mr. E. Benjamin (Hart and
Benjamin, Solicitors), Collins-street, Melbourne;
Thomas Robinson, Carlton, Melbourne ; Mrs. J.
Love, 145 Johnston-street, Collingwood, were all
promptly cured of rheumatism by the same means.
Such things to the well, mentally, were not diver-
sions, but how readily they were vanquished.

Medical.
Medical Reform.
THERE exists a crying need for the medi-
cal profession to stay the quackery
which is so rampant in these colonies. Men
having no medical qualifications nor aptitude
for the treatment of diseases, still advertise
with impunity as “doctors”; and guarantee
to do what they know they are incapable of
doing, and thus deceive the public who are
caught by their specious and lying advertise-
ments, as is amply testified by reading the ex-
posures in the
DAILY TELEGRAPH
from August 3 to 11, 1887.
A chief cause of this state of things is that
medical men from mistaken notions of profes-
sional decorum, and because they consider it
“infra dig,” refrain from entering the field
against these quacks. So long as this prudery
exists so long will this canker consume our
youth. At the risk, however, of professional
opprobrium I have decided to throw aside this
false delicacy, and avow my sympathy with,
and readiness to treat such affections, in the
hope of saving the young of both sexes from
the ruin of health and mind which consulting
these quacks ensures, not to mention the ruin
of their pockets.
“ A patient writes to me : “ I am in such a
miserable state I would pay you what I could
afford for a lifetime, if you could possibly be
the means of letting me know what health is,
and perhaps saving me from suicide which
seems to be my only release if this appeal
fails. Kindly and humanely do not allow it to
be in vain, and you will never regret it. I
have been victimised by a person calling
HIMSELF A ‘ DOCTOR.
Now, if qualified medical men would adver-
tise in such a way as to bring their names be-
fore these sufferers, and convince them of their
sympathy, cases like the above, which unfortu-
nately are of daily occurrence, would not
occur ; groundless fears would not be excited,
and where disease exists it would be safely
and permanently cured.
I am a legally and highly-qualified
Practitioner, and have for over TWENTY
YEARS DEVOTED ESPECIAL STUDY
to
Chronic, Nervous and Special Diseases
OF
Men and Women,
and can, therefore, undertake to GUARAN-
TEE A CURE IN ALL CASES.
£3T Sufferers from Impaired Vitality, or
Nervousness, should read my hook on
“NERVOUS DEBILITY,”
Free by Post for Is.,
where they will find much useful information
and directions for
SELF-CURE.
Those wishing a farther insight should read
my work on the “Male Generative Func-
tion in Health and Disease,” free by post

At Poverty Point.
Apropos of the “Formosa” revival, a Melbourne
weekly (whose critic is apt to get hopelessly lost m
reminiscences) mentions that John Bilhngton was the
original Sam Boker. The present writer, hav * n S *
fairly intelligent infant at the tune, is prepared to swear
that John Bouse played the part in question. Mrs. BU-
lington—a rough and ready lady who could run histuomo
rings round her husband, as John B. would doubtless be
the first to admit -represented Sam Boker s wife, as it
happened. The cast of “ Formosa was especially good
all through. Irving was the Compton Kerr, whose an-
nouncement of his name was met by Mrs. Bilhngton s
enquiry—" How do you spell it? —an idea which has
been cribbed by a dozen dramatists since Boucicault first
struck it. He couldn’t have got that from the I rench.
The bibulous Brittain Wright was the loafer with an
eternal “ little dawg ” to sell, and clever David. Fisher had
a part in “ the Majah ” which suited him to the life. 1 ooi
David is dead enough now, as are Wright, Rouse, and
others of the old crowd.
The weakest point about David Christie Murray’s
“ Chums” is said to be its bookiuess : its characters have
a weakness for coming down to the footlights and talking
by the yard, while the crowd in the background swell the
numbers of the unemployed. Then the first act is the
strongest, the third act the weakest of the three. As toi
the story, an Auckland critic gushingly says ; It is one
of the tenderest and most pathetic little things that ever
emanated from the brain of a novelist —a very poem m
prose. A story that must touch the hearts and move the
spirits of all, by reason of its powerful humanity ; a story
of passionate love contending with truest honour, ot
childish love and confidence, of tenderest pity and sincerest
sympathy.”
Harper’s Monthly, June, brings to mind a romantic
elopement of yore, in w'hich two Australians
were concerned —W. S. Lyster and Mrs. W. S. Lyster,
professionally known as Georgia Hudson, the Pocahontas
in that famous burlesque of John Broughams, at the
New York Olympic, when he acted Powhattan, and
Waleot was his companion in drollery, A la Snazelle and
Leitch. Harper gives an engraving of Brougham and

It is stated that the cause of Gilbert and Sullivan’s
apparently hopeless falling-out was a squabble about a
roll of carpet. This must be untrue. No two men of
equal position and opulence ever fell out for such a trum-
pery cause. If you hate a man you seize the first con-
venient pretext for quarrelling with him. The carpet is a
mere pretext.

The experimental matinee is now the only manner in
which the unknown British author can gain the ear of the
public, or ensure an after run of his work if successful. A
matinee production never costs less than a hundred
pounds, sometimes twice the amount.

proved he interfered with nobody’s rights, and was privi-
leged with an introduction to the Virgin Mary, the
Burgomaster’s daughter.

Vivier, the once-famous French-horn player, was so un-
nerved by Lord Houghton’s blowing his nose at a critical
moment of his performances, that he threw up all his
London engagements. “Ah,” he would say after this,
“ the English have terrible noses; they remind you of the
Day of Judgment.”
Nemo Dramatic Club lately produced “ Pygmalion and
Galatea,” at Sydney Royal Standard, to a tolerably full
house. It is almost needless to say that W. S. Gilbert
didn’t dive into mythology in the interests of this club
—had he done so he would certainly have introduced a
few horse-ebupers and boarding-house slaveys into the
cast. Mrs. W. E. Sheridan (Louise Davenport) as Galatea
played effectively and looked very “ fetching,” but the
weak-kneedness and woodenness of the supporting
amateurs made the whole performance most depressing.
It is understood that the next effort of the club will be
melo-drama, namely, “Sweeny Todd, the Demon Barber.”
The St. James’ Gazette says that “ alike for beauty of
voice and perfection of style Madame Melba is certainly
the first artiste of the present day.”

tary, a ridiculous person, is named Mitford. As this
happens to be also the name of an estimable man who in
real life has acted in that capacity to four ministers, the
dramatist has made a change.

understood to view with veneration.
George Grossmith left the Savoy Theatre after a twelve
years’ engagement, chiefly, he says, because he could not
endure the monotony of the long runs. That's all very
well, but if Grossmith has not more originality than the
usual single-handed showman his life will be still more
monotonous now. Were it not for the introduction of a
young wife, Monologuist Maccabe would be playing from
exactly the same bill to-day that -he hung out over 20
years ago at the Egyptian Hall.

modesty in a dance that sets her very little behind Kale
Vaii»'haii. She is, however, doubtless an American, from
her lunch-eulogised delivery of an American dialect poem.
Her mother has refused for her many music-hall engage-
ments, and allows her to perform in drawing-room enter-
tainments only, at present at all events.

On the morning after the first performance of Con-
ductor Cowen’s “Scandinavian” Symphony in London
Dr. Louis Engel (the now notorious seducer), then musical
critic for the World, wrote the following suggestive
note:— “Dear Fred,—A question— frank, as usual.
Would you prefer me to say nothing about your concert,
or to speak my mind freely about your Symphony ? I
candidly tell you Ido not like it. Tell me which you
prefer.—Yours truly, L. Enqbl.” Cowen declined to take
the hint and curtly replied: “Write just, what you
please.”

Jefferson, in bis autobiography, writes of Charles Kean,
and meeting him in Melbourne. During Keans fust
week of acting, Jefferson met him in Albert Park one
morning, sitting on a bench, and gazing disconsolately
towards the ocean. Kean said he was sorry he had come,
for he and his wife were too old, Barry Sullivan damaged
them bv persistently acting the same plays, at the Royal,
after he had announced that his liberal offers must in-
fallibly bring Mr. and Mrs. Kean and G. V. Brooke to ins
theatre. Kean said his wife buoy ed him up, but, in achat
with her, Jefferson found she was much distressed. He
pays a high tribute to her, as a lady and an actress.

John Norton, the Protectionist candidate
for Hartley, N.S.W., has been defeated by 31 ! I
votes by John Hurley, whom financial difficulties
forced before his constituents again. This election
has not been (as is assumed by the Foreign-trade
press) a true test as to the relative popularity of
Protection and what in N.S.W. is impudently
called Freetrade. There is no more off-handed
and personally popular man in Australia than
Hurley, who is thoroughly well-known and well-
liked in the district, even by the Protectionists ;
while his opponent was, comparatively speaking,
a stranger, with little or no money at his back,
and who, as Ins candidature was for several
reasons resented by the National Club, re-
ceived but lukewarm support from the
leaden of the party he ostensibly represented.
The fact that Mr. Norton had, on a somewhat
memorable political occasion, acted for Sydney E,
News in the constituency and had done the work
of that shifty and ungraspable Foreign trade
paper with apparent enthusiasm considerably dis-
counted his chances. Then, Norton is a newly-
converted and fanatical teetotaller ; while Hurley,
who concedes the right of every citizen to take his
beer, and denies the right of the publicans to State
compensation, strikes a fair balance between the
liquor-disputants. The Parkesites have managed
to get (in the main) the teetotal vob> though
believing not in teetotalism.

FREETRADE or PROTECTION.
Some vote Protection, some Freetrade,
As best for this young country’s weal.
But ask the youth or blushing maid .
And each will answer — PemelL’s Meal !
As best, because it has been tried,
There’s nothing half so good beside.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
’Tis good and pure’and cheap, I wean.
The Genesis of the Australian Buffalo.
Dear Bulletin, —What glorious sport I used to
think buffalo-hunting, when Captain Mayne
Reid was my boyhood’s author, and, in fancy, I
roamed with him over boundless prairies, living
on buffalo-hump ! When 1 came to hunt buffaloes
in North Australia I found it, however, quite a
different matter. The first I tumbled on I took
for a bunyip, and so did my horse, for he turned
and sprinted for half-a-mile ere it was possible to
remonstrate with him. The object of his alarm
was the animal known in India as the “swamp-
buffalo,” and life in Australia has,not improved
him. He’s a mean-looking beast, with a pair of
preposterous horns, fixed on to a body .about the
size of a boar built on a liberal and long-legged
scale. Further, he appears to be slightly imbe-
cile, and when the sun gets hot he goes and soaks
his head in the nearest mud-hole, and, when dis-
turbed, starts up and looks around with bloodshot
■eyes and half-a-load of grass or weeds hanging
round his head. As an immigrant he first came to
the colony in 1825, and was introduced to Mel-
ville Island, and 'Raffles Bay on the mainland,
where a few were left to shift for themselves
after the departure of their white sponsors.
Melville Island is now pretty well stocked
with buffaloes, but shooting-parties who ad venture
there from Port Darwin find their sport rather
interfered with by the natives, who, ever since the
days of King, the„navigator, have been remarkable
for aggressiveness. Somehow the buffaloes and
the aborigines have lived on distantly-speaking
terms, probably on account of a. similarity of
colour, but since the abandonment of Fort Dundas
(in 182!)) no white settlement has been formed on
the island, and only an occasional visit has been
paid thereto by shooting-parties. At Raffles Bay
and Port Essington, the two spots where settle-
ment was attempted and buffaloes acclimatised,
the unfortunate quadruped has not led such an
easy-going existence. Sometimes people are
assured that a fortune lies in the smoking and
curing of buffalo-humps, tongues, and skins, and
then he is pursued and slain forthwith ; but after
awhile, the bold hunter gets tired and then the
buffalo is once more left in peace. But there’s
none of the Mayne Reid business about it. No
headlong gallops side by side over the billowy
prairie, firing into the lordly leaders of the herd.
Hunting the buffalo in North Queensland is not a
bit like that. You’ve got to mess about to get
within shot, and kneel down on a green-ants’ nest
to take aim ; and, if you hit anything, you must
hump the proceeds over miles and miles of devil-
devil country.
At the same time that the buffaloes were left
to the tender mercies of a strange continent
'a number of Timor ponies were also abandoned,
and they likewise have thriven in a small
and modest manner. By the same token, two
convicts also took their departure at the time of
the evacuation : but apparently they did not
increase and multiply, for nothing has been heard
of them from that day to this. The Timor pony
of the north of Arnheim’s Land is nearly as un-
satisfactory as the buffalo in appearance. He is
larger than a big Newfoundland dog, but not
much ; he is generally an albino, and if he wants
to get rid of you he does not buck, but stops,
ducks his head, and draws back suddenly, and
you find yourself unexpectedly sitting on the
ground with a cream-coloured fiend with fiery
pink eyes gazing sternly at you. The buffa’oes
have not sprei d widely, 1 1 icy filled up a bit of spare
country in the neighbourhood of Pert Essington,
and then stopped. Single animals, generally bulls
which have been chivied tint of the mob, find their
way into other parts and take up with cattle for a
bit. One travelled right round the Cfulf of Car-
pentaria to the Queensland border; and they have
been seen and their horns found in the blacks’
camps low down on the Daly River —towards the
mouth of which there is now splendid alligator-
shooting. There is also a legend that a buffalo
started down the overland telegraph. He got
nearly as far as Tennant’s Creek Station, when he
was unexpectedly met by a repairing-party, one
of whom was up a pole at the time. Everybody
cleared out, leaving the unfortunate man a l one.
When they reached the station and told their
wondrous tale, the Adelaide people were shocked
by receiving a message stating : “Ihe devil ap-
proaching, has already taken a linesman. Be pre-
pared.” Prayers were offered up and parsons were
jubilant at the prospect of vanquishing the evil one,
when the news arrived that the missing linesman
had turned up, and that the devil had turned off
towards Queensland.
* # *
Some thirteen years ago a company,
calling themselves the Coburg Cattle Com-
pany, after the name of the Coburg peninsula on
which Port Essington stands, took up the adjacent
land and attempted to civilise the buffaloes with a
view to the hump-and-tongue-curing business.
They*were partially successful; some 300 buffaloes
were mustered and quietened ; but expenses were
heavy and times depressed, so active work had to
be abandoned. Since then buffalo-shooting has
been carried on in a desultory kind of manner by
anybody who liked to take to it. This reminds
me that the other day I saw a station offered for
sale in the Northern Territory with 20,000
buffaloes on it. Now, I always believe an adver-
tisement because I never read one yet that did
not contain the calm, naked truth; but foY all
that when I pur base that station I shall insist
upon delivery being given, and decline to take it
over on a book-muster. Down in Western Aus-
tralia, till; other day, a man offered a-bit of
country for sale, and as a recommendation, said
there were at'least a thousand kangaroos on it.
Shades of old Heft hunters,. listen to that as a
recommendation'! Very soon we shaft have an
estate held up for admiration as being suitable for
stud-rabbit breeding purposes. On a whole
the Australian buffalo, then, is a fraud; there’s
“no romance to be got out of him, and
he’s npt. a bit picturesque in appearance.
He can get in a rage sometimes, however. ' One
of them treed a blackfellow once and kept
him there .all day. After dark he slipped down
and ran for it, but the buffalo, who was wounded,
caught him. There’s a sort of wombat up in that
country that burrows in the ground and digs
considerable holes, treacherous at times. Into
one of these the nigger tumbled, and the buffalo
fell on top of him and broke his own neck. But he
punched the life out of the unfortunate native, and
their bodies were pulled out together. The Far-
Northern buffalo-hunters utilize the lazy blacks to
go with them and carry back the skins, &c. Until
a man carries a raw hide and a hump in it he has
no idea how much profanity he has in him.—
Yours, F. E.—Port Darwin, N.T., June>B. .
The Land of the Crocodile.
When Governor Norman and his Ministers
were in Cairns on a recent occasion, Donaldson,
the Colonial Treasurer, was interviewed about
midivght in the ball-room's banqueting-room by a
band of leading citizens, compliance with whose
modest demands would have disposed of the next
three-million loan in one ac". While Donaldson
was calculating the probable consequences of
murdering the whole deputation, a genial old
gentleman cheerfully remarked that “nothing
would be obtained from the Treasurer until they
filled him up with whisky ! ” It so happens that
Donaldson never drinks whisky, but he said, “If
I chose to take whisky I could put any man in
the district under the table ! ” The beard of the
old gentleman fairly curled with indignation at
this reckless imputation on the capacity of the
district. “By Heavens,” he said, “1 will under-
take to booze you up myself ! ” Then he sent a
messenger round to all the eleven hotels to know
how much stuff was in stock among the lot.
About four o’clock in the morning he was heard
informing the other “leading citizens” that
“ thersh shonly shix dray-loadsh whishky in sha
townsh —whash the yoosh o’ that! ”
# # *
Among the present Queensland Ministry
are two strict cold-water men—Donaldson, the
Treasurer, and Powers, the Minister for Educa-
tion. There are also two others who are currently
believed to have taken over the cold-water men’s
total liabilities.
* * *
When Miss Van Tassel, the balloonist,
took a fly-round over Maryborough, she descended
in a field belonging to a German farmer. This
Teutonic agriculturist was engaged at the time
chopping out a stump with an American axe ;
his frau, clad in the simple-garments of the female
peasant, was about 100 yards away gracefully
digging sweet potatoes with her feet. Perceiving
a dark shadow sweeping over the scene the Teuton
looked up to see what unusual bird was soaring
round tbe locality, and there he beheld the beauti-
ful Van Tassel (in tights) seated on the cross-bar
of the parachute and gracefully descending from
the clouds. Believing this to be an angel sent
down direct to scoop him up, and feeling totally
unprepared for the occasion, Hans flung away his
axe and started to break bis previous records in a
race, for home, at the same time yelling out,
several octaves above the yell of a lost new-chum,
“ Adi"! mein Gott, not yet, not yet, dere vash
muin frau-Qver dere; you gets ayay mit her !” And
the frail, with one foot buried in a hillock of pota-
toes, looked on with stolid indifference, merely
remarking; “I yonder vat de tyvil dat vash,
anvhow !”

During Governor Norman’s visit to one of
the northern sea-coast towns, a local alderman,
who took a leading part among the refreshments
all through the programme, was observed gazing
pensively at a glassful of toothpicks on the banquet
table. Then he turned to Baden Powell, the
Governor’s private secretary, and confidentially
asked him if he knew “what the blazes all them
quill pens is for ? ”
* * *
Stuart Russell, author of the “ Genesis of
Queensland,” was once 'coming down from Cecil
Plains station to Brisbane, when lie was suddenly
bailed up for money by a tall, powerful myall at
the Old Man Waterhole.” Russell produced a
pistol, much to the surprise of the nigger, who
thought he was unarmed. But the bushranger
was a myall of great self-possession and resource.
Finding a sudden change of front to be urgently
necessary, he. threw up bis hands apologetically
and called out “baal ! baal ! me pa;/ pan ! ” and
forthwith from a long coil of very dirty rag he
unrolled a penny, placed it in Russell’s hand, and
forthwith made dust and leaves fly ere vanishing
into the scrub, leaving Russell fit to roll off the
saddle with laughter.
* # *
A certain Queensland vice-regal attache
has a playful weakness for barking and crowing at
a banquet during periods of applause. This .eccen-
tric Plantagenet asked a sarcastic Queensland girl
at a ball recently if she heard “ his musical inter-
jection at the supper?” “I did, Captain,”replied
the fair daughter of the North, “and it struck me
that you -wouldn't make cither a second-rate
retriever or a third-class rooster !” Then there was
a lull in the conversation.
# # #
Some time ago Judge Pope Cooper was
travelling bn the coast north of Townsville, and
stayed for supper at a bush'hotel. The publican
had received intimation after dark that the judge
would be in to supper about ten o’clock. He had
no fowls of his own, and there were none to be
bought; in fact, the only poultry in the locality
was possessed by a rival publican about fifty
yards away, who owned, among others, a big
Brahma rooster, whose evening perch was on a
scrub-tree in the back-yard. This bird was seized
in the midst of slumber, his dying voice stifled by
rude hands, and at ten o’clock that night he was
served up to Judge Cooper with caper sauce and
baked sweet potatoes, washed down with cham-
pagne. Next day the owner of the bird was
offering a pound for the scalp of the native dog, or
fifty pounds for the Chinaman ! He had never
even caught a sniff of the burning feathers ! The
judge would do well to carry his own fowls, as
well as his own champagne, when travelling
among the pioneers of an advanced civilisation.
MAROOGALINE.

HOGMANAY IN GERMAN.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was a forlorn party of teamsters who were en-
camped one New Year’s Eve, some 17 years ago,
at the Lanco Crossing, on the road between Cape
Shorter and Gumgum. The rains had come weeks
before their usual time, and the Lanco was up a
banker. They were on the Turretpan side of the
Lanco, and they had no grog. Most of them were
of Scotch extraction and this was Hogmanay ! No
wonder they were gloomy.
I was then in a billet which involved my fre-
quent travelling between Tingaling—at a “skew-
off ’ from Gumgum—and Merivale, a station about
60 miles equidistant from Cape Shorter and Gum-
gum. On this particular evening, I arrived from
Merivale at Turretpan, which overhangs the
Lanco, with a knooked-up horse, named Boko, and
with full determination to reach Gumgum before
the New Year had fairly dawned. The owner of:
Turretpan was known as “Cranky Rodwaite,”
and was a perfect terror to most travellers, on
account of his surliness and inhospitality. Erudite
himself, he lived the life of a recluse, so far as a
station-owner living on a public track could act
the hermit. He had a beautiful wife whom
visitors saw only at a distance, and who was con-
sequently exalted by imagination into a perfect
Cleopatra. Rodwaite had a mania for German
servants, though he was himself an Englishman.
His stockman was German —and a very poor
stockman. His “ married couple ” were Germans.
Nothing but German was ever spoken in the Rod-
waite household; nothing but German on the
station, except when uni-lingo’d visitors compelled
Rodwaite to utter the hated Anglo-Saxon. I had
gained his favour by listening several times, with
an intelligent face—smiling when the inflections of
his voice hinted that I ought to, and looking glum
or wrathful according to inspiration—to long
harangues in German as if I understood it,
whereas I have but the schoolboy’s smatterings of
the simplest Teutonic sentences. So I had actually
once dined en famille with Rodwaite —and, by the
way, had to pretend to have “fever ’n aiger in
order to avoid answering in German.
Being anxious to get on to Gumgum on this par-
ticular New Year’s Eve, I went straight to Rod-
waite and asked him to lend me a horse. He was
in an amiable mood, to judge by his face, but he
replied with a lot of gutturals which I understood
to be queries as to why I didn’t prefer my request
in German, and then he indicated something with
a sweep of his hand which I boldly interpreted
into the gracious answer that I might “ take
Trumpeter from the home paddock.” On spec., I
went and caught Trumpeter, transferred poor
Boko’s saddle and bags to him, and it must have
been all right, for Rodwaite wished me a distinct
“(/tiien abend.”
I rode straight down a steepish track to the Lanco
Crossing and found the river right up. I suppose
Rodwaite had warned me—in German-that such
would be the case. Here I came across four sets
of teamsters, eight men in all, who immediately
swarmed on me with queries relative to Hog-
manay. Alas ! I could not help them, but re-
membered that a few miles back I had overtaken
Jim Macpot, the hawker, who was going to camp
at this very “the nicht.” I told them
this, and since I had wasted so much time in bor-
rowing a horse in German, I guessed that Macpot
was “ close up.” Presently his waggon came
round the base of the hill, he having taken a lower
track—probably to avoid Cranky Rodwaite. We
let him come right on and turn out his one moke.
Then we rushed his tilt,
“Got any grog?” , ,
“ Not a blessed drop; sold out the last bottle of
brandy at Whiteman’s to the caretaker.”
“ anv pain-killer?”
“ No.”
“ Got any ohlorodyne?”
“No.” i • *
“Why, you’ve not got anything ! And this is
Hogmanay !’’
“ Go and ask Cranky Rodwaite for some.’
“ He’s a teetotaller; go yourself.”
“ I had, meanwhile, been rummaging over Mac-
pot’s stores, and suddenly cried, “ He’s got a
easeful of scents, boys. All scents are made with
pure rectified spirits !”
“ Trot’em out. ”
And out those scents were trotted-eau-de-
cologne, lavender waters, opopouaxes, “juices of
Mount Hermon,” and the Old Scratch hiinse f
knows what else. The teamsters bought Macpot
clean out.
All the perfumeries were poured into a bucket
which a moment before had been ■ mptily hanging
under one cf the drays; the bucket itself was
swung over the lire, and a hot jorum there and
then compounded, sugar being added to give it a
relish. One super-artistic brewer added some
“ Berwick’s Baking Powder,” to, as he said, ‘ put
a head on it! ”
With this nauseous compound—it was indeed
detestable ; it makes me ill even now to think of
it—we celebrated Hogmanay, and sang “ Auld
Lang Syne ” and did many other signs and won-
ders. We got “fou eneuch ”in very short space.
Soon, however, I began to think of swimming
Trumpeter over the flooded Lanco crossing, as the
sun’s rays were levelling. Witli my faithful old
Boko, it had always been my habit to strip naked,
tie my clothes high up swag-like on the pommel
of the saddle, lead him in—he was a splendid and
a nerveful swimmer, of pure Anchorite breed-
put the reins under an armpit and go ahead
slantingly. I seen many men swim horses
by holding on to their tails. I never could under-
stand the trick of it, though I tried several times,
so I stuck to my Boko method whenever I had to
tempt the flood.
I tried it on with Trumpeter. Now, whether it
was the perfumery, or some trick- on the part of
the ranting Scots, or Trumpeter’s own cusseducss,
I never knew. But the brute broke away from
me somehow directly he lost bottom, plunged back
to the Turretpan bank, and raced up the Turretpan
hill as fast as he could head.
And he had all my duds on the saddle ! And
the top si ip rail was always left down, I knew !!
I asked the jovial opoponax-drinkers to lend
me some clothes —“only a pair of breeks, for the
love of heaven”—in order to go after the horse.
I beseeched, I cursed, I threatened, I raved, I
wept. The teamsters wouldn’t; Macpot, the
hawker, wouldn’t. The joke, they said, was too
good to spoil!
In despair, I started up tlie hill, naked as the
most innocent savage. I ran all I knew, in order
to get there, maybe, before anyone observed
Trumpeter's return.
No use. The cantankerous moke was parading
proudly in front of the home verandah with Rod-
waite and Mrs. Rodwaite looking down at him !
If he had been a trained and performing circus-
horse, he couldn't have fixed up a hotter situation.
I rushed at the position, sprang recklessly on his
back, yelled out something about “ calling only for
mv clothes,” and raced him downhill again full
split.
Arrived at the camp exhausted with the ordeal,
I threw myself on the ground and called loudly
for more opoponax-mixture. Then I related to
the others the details of naked intrusion, and—we
had more opoponax-mixture. Suddenly we heard
a step and saw Cranky Rod waite, wrathful and
malignant, and bridle in hand. Hurling some
German epithets at me, he took my bridle off
Trumpeter, put on his own, oast off my saddle
and bags to the earth, and turned to lead the
moke away.
Then up sprang I —“ What shall we do with
him, boys? Duck him? Strip him and escort
him home ? ”
“Gie him some Hogmanay, * suggested one.
The idea was fire-fat to us. Around that un-
fortunate man we ten howling, Hogmanaying
fiends circled, threateningly—one holding out a
pannikin of “ Hogmanay,” and all declaring that
wo wouldn’t let him go till daylight next morningif
he didn't drink it.
Ho drank it. What sort of German he
scrambled out afterwards, we couldn’t tell for
laughing at the grimaces he was making, and the
manner in which he contorted and writhed las
body. So we let him go.
I stayed at the Lanoo Crossing all that night,
and early next morning went up to Turret pan,
bridle in hand, and a swelling in the head, to
catch Boko. I had just got him when Rod waite
came along and confronted me. I was too sick
with opoponax to “sass back,” or analyse
his eloquence. The German gutturals fell in
thunderstorms of artillery from his lips ; the
modulations of his passion ranged from the roar of
the sweeping blast to the shriek of the cyclone’s
fringe; but I only looked at him stupidly and
meekly. When he stopped for breath, I, from a
stump, got on birebacked Boko. Again his voice
reverberated with Teutonic thunder. I kept on
gazing dazedly at him. At last —and in English! —
“Can’t you understand German?”
“No.”
“Then”—a mighty pause, in the vain effort to
clutch sufficient invective, and a clawing at no-
thing in the air—“then, Damn !”
I rode do wn the hill.

There are great masses of gloom in these
colonies since the news arrived that Carnarvon
had departed to join the procession which flits
incessantly through the misty golden door ; and
despair is lying around in immense hunks and
conspicuous blobs of grief are everywhere. Several
hundred people have gone into mourning on the
unhappy occasion and are a prey to corroding
care. They were all persons who were intimate
with the lamented Carnarvon when he visited
"Australia a year or two ago, and used to drop in
and see him every time they were passing. He
looked on the whole lot of them as a brother,
and he frequently urged them to run over to
England and take pot-luck in his ancestral
halls and be introduced to the family. They
all spent many happy evenings with the deceased.
and when the night was sultry Carnarvon used to
prop the window open with his coronet, or the
title-deeds of his vast estates, with an unobtrusive
affability that was touching in the extreme ; and
then he would order up the drinks, and tell them
to make themselves at home. Four hundred and
twenty two of them were the most intelligent in-
dividuals the late earl ever met, and the entire
crowd habitually addressed him by his Christian
name. Most of them gave him some valuable hints
which he promised to embody in an Act of Parlia-
ment whenever he got home, but unhappily Death
came in and cut him off just as he was about to
begin. He frequently stated that if he had known
375 of them when he was young his political career
might have been very different, and there were B'J
in particular who, he felt sure, would stand by
him in any emergency. They all feel now that his
confidence was not misplaced. Also, there were
, enough of them to fill eight omnibuses to whom he
mentioned regretfully that he wished they could
take his place in politics aft°r he was gone, and
the grief of this lot is something too inexpressible
to mention. When an ordinary person flies it is
a common thing for him to “cast a gloom, but
the shade that Carnarvon has cast over thi s country
is altogether too large for words.
Old Hegarty, who leased the ancient Syd-
ney (Queen’s Theatre for some years, was prone to
dabble in speculations which were not always
successful. A friend cf his once induced him to
buy an infallible egg-preserving composition which
he (the friend) had just invented, pointing out
that through eggs lay the way to fortune, for they
were only ninepence a dozen just then, while in
the winter months their selling price was at least
half-a-crown. Hegarty espoused the idea with
unction and bought up every egg in Sussex-street
without delay, and turned the theatre for a time into
a produce store, where he and his assistants were
for ever dipping eggs into some mysterious liquid,
and thenre-casingthem. This mighthavebeengoing
on till now, were it not that the first-purchased
eggs soon began to odorise so lustily that Eggarty
suddenly suspended operations and came very near
suspending his friend, the inventor. As for the
eggs that remained fresh, or partially fresh, he,
with the assistance of his company, speedily ate
them, but the bad Tins he didn’t know what to do
with. They were fast ruining the name of the
theatre, too; when the curtain rose o’ nights,
the audience in the stalls usually rose, too,
and the stench was so powerful that the Ghost in
“Hamlet,” usually a ponderous and very palpable
person, might have been wafted along on it with
the aid of a blacksmith’s bellows at a distance of
at least three feet from the ground. Just then an
alligator came to town and Hegarty, hearing that
the favourite food of the saurian in captivity was
hen-fruit, went round and sold a number of oases
to the owner at a loss of scent per cent, on first
cost. Returning, after xn interval of some
days, in order to trade off the remainder of
his" stock, he was compelled to force an entrance
through a strong effluvium which was ringing the
bell and taking money at the door. Inside he
found the alligator in convulsions, while the pro-
prietor, with tears in his eyes, was applying mus-
tard to the pit of the unfortunate animal’s stomach.
The eggs had been a death-blow to the Queensland
native, and while Hegarty contemplated his pur-
chase with a view to his compulsory engulphment
of an article somewhat out of fashionable use, his
spirit passed peacefully away one afternoon just as
the inspector of nuisances was wrangling with the
owner of its scaly tenement about the shameful
state of the premises.

Ibsen and His Critics.
Dear Bulletin,— “ Marriage is a hard way of
earning one’s living ! ” This remark I once over-
heard from a conversation between two married
women touching the blessings and curses of the
marriage conventional. It sounds cynical to the
ears of the “ Mrs. Generals ’ of everyday bread-
and-butter existence, content with skating on the
safe edges of life's ice-floe, who shut their eyes to,
or overlook witli a purblind insistence all that
they do not desire to sec. Considered as a mode
of earning one’s living, marriage is one of the
hardest. The billet of the small'domestic and
petty petticoat appanage to a man’s fuller and
mo re vine life is neither advantageous to the
prospects nor ennobling to the character of the
permanent retainer of little more than a mans
name with the respectable prefix of “Mrs.” _ Iho
bond of union is usually nothing beyond a signa-
turo to a formal-looking bit of paper. a plain band
of meaningless metal upon a certain finger- of the
left-hand - emblematic of the left-handed relation-
ship of the average married woman to the pio-
blems and pleasures and penalties of life and
little beside. Woman married in the purely con-
ventional sense, is so much tangible 1 jo*
perty, a sort of funded echo who holds
• omewhat the position of the Greek slave
advanced to be the pasha’s viz.er, petted
and played with when the tyrant is in a
good humour, calmly put aside as a thing of
no meaning, a costly toy of little use, when the
veal contingencies of life arise, obtaining favours
by wiles, and power by studying the weaknesses
and pandering to the vices of the lord and master,
if not altogether,of life and death,, at least of life
and happiness. Woman pliys the part, in the
conventional marriage, of a doll, a plaything,
something which must attune itself to the mood or
the caprice of somebody who loves to play with
dolls, to dress dolls, to exult in their waxen
beauty with all the pride of proprietorship, and to
lock them up securely when wearied of the play.
Sometimes the doll disappears in the drudge; but
not often does the wife hold her head erect as a
being whose life is no less to her than that of the
man to him. Not often do we find a man who asks
for a mate a human being, thinking her own
thoughts (with a right to them), responsible for
her own actions, the architect of her own life; and
all the insanity of woman’s-rights, female fran-
chise, et hoc genus orine, is the blind, mayhap
foolish protest of women who feel that
there is something wrong without knowing the
real error of their position. Social philosophers
and psychological novelists, and dramatists in
morbid pathology refer the trouble to various
manifestations of that which cynics sneer at under
the name of love, and moralists denounce as im-
pure passion, and police-court reporters allude to
in descriptive epithets of many varieties of slang.
Everywhere amongst the most civilised commu-
nities is evidenced a vague unrest at the position
held- by woman in the marriage relationship.
Everywhere legislatures have brought under con-
sideration the necessity of extending the limita-
tions of the divorce-laws, and during the past yeai
or so Europe has been stirred to its social depths
by the performance of Henrik Ibsen’s “A Doll’s
House,” and more recently by the publication of
Tolstoi’s “Kreutzer Sonata,” and the alleged awful
drama by the same author, “La Puissance des
Tenebres,” produced simultaneously at Paris and
St. Petersburg. All these works deal with the
marriage problem as it is presented in modern
highly-civilised and conventional society. Tolstoi
writes as an Oriental who denies love, as a pessi-
mist who considers that paternity is a crime, and
regards as eminently desirablethe winding-up of the
human race on aee unit of its being a dark and
disastrous failure to discover what life means after
scienee-knows-not-how-many centuries of failure.
This is the teaching of “La Puissance de Tene-
bres. ’ Ibsen, on the contrary, cynic, enthusiast,
and reformer as he is, sees ahead to a possible per-
fectibility attainable only by the wholesale demo-
lition of conventions, the elevation of labour, and
the freedom, mental and moral, of woman. In
drama after drama he has dealt with phase upon
phase of what is rather vaguely called the “Woman
Question.” In the “Doll's House’’—just now a
bone of contention amongst the newspaper critics
of wheresoever the play has been produced, Sydney
included —he insists upon the necessity of the
ideal union of man and wife—if the race would
have any sort of healthy continuity of any kind
whatsoever. This, too, he does in the most scien-
tific manner, in a style which is cold as ice and
pure as snow. He wastes not a single word.
Every sentence tells, and the work is as cleanly
cut as an anatomical study reproduced in plaster
by a master of the modeller’s art. if the play is
read with the mind awake and all its faculties at
full tension, one conclusion is forced upon the
reader—the absolute need for the freedom of
woman, intellectually and morally ; of the volun-
tary union, which must also be the sum
of all the forces which find their adequate
expression in the marriage resting on other
than upon modern conventional grounds. But
what, after all, is the “Doji’s House?” We
find a great deal of stress laid by Old-Mother-
Grundyish critics, or criticasters and cpiticlmgs,
upon a “stocking” incident. This sort of thing
is beneath contempt, and matches well with the
Superficial “propriety” which is spread, like a
very thin layer of rancid butter on a very coarse
piece of bread, over the whole of life’s three-and-
sixpence per gallon copal-varnished veneer. The
incident in question is perfectly natural, and
emphasises Nora’s absolute perturbability of mind,
her ignorance of what is passing around her, and
the absorbing anxiety with which she contem-
plates the crux of her position. But what, after
all, is the “Doll’s House?” Here it is in a
Sentence of Jean Paul Richter’s -. —
But, before and after being a mother, one is a human
being; and neither the motherly nor the wifely destina-
tion can overbalance or replace the human, hut must
become its means, not its end.
This is the sum and substance of all that Ibsen
so artistically presents to ns in a really epoch-
making study. One do not call it a drama,
a play. It is a sketch, a psychological dis-
section in dialogue of a very high order. It may
not amuse. Does JEschylus amuse, or Goethe?
Does Shakespeare amuse, or Victor Hugo ?
These are the giants of the theatre. Calderon is
terrible. The magnificent Elizabethan drama, of
which the lightest achievement is a colossus over-
towering a pigmy compared with anything done
in the English literature of the stage since its grand
death-agony in Shirley and Ford and Otway, is
stuffed with tragedy of a sombre and awesome
grandeur. Yet we are told that the province of
the playwright is to amuse. In other words, we
have, at the summit of our civilisation, grown so
small that we can in cold blood consecrate two,
three, four hours of our night’s existence to inane
giggling. Ibsen doesn’t giggle, and can make
people think only! Herein lies the reason that
he must be hounded off a stage which unfolds its
spacious wings for the harbourage of leggy inde-
cencies and burnt-cork banalities. Consider the
“Doll’s House,” and the lesson of it. A wife,
brought up—as thousands upon thousands of
women all around us are brought up—to fill the
part of a sort of domestic female jeste*, echoing
the genteel vulgarities of a conventional, narrow
albeit gentlemanly husband, who is crammed
with selfishness and high-flown commonplaces
of no particular meaning, commits a crime—as
crime is regarded in social circles. In other
words, her husband is ill and poor, and her father
is dying. The doctors tell her that only change
to a wanner climate than that of Norway—ltaly,
for example—can save him. Money is needed !
Her father is too ill to be consulted in the matter.
Her father must die; she is confronted with the
possibility of losing also the husband she so dearly
loves. Goaded by the purest affection she forges
her father’s name to a document by which this so
necessary money may be obtained. Had her
father been in a condition to sign this docu-
ment she knows well that he would have done
so. Here begins the tragedy ! The forgery is
discovered by the man who advances the money.
The sole object of her life is to keep this discovery
from her husband’s knowledge. Not because she
fears to face the consequences of her crime. Oh,
no! She doesn’t even know it is a crime. Did
she not do this thing from pure love? Was it not
her father’s name that she forged ? Nevertheless
society is inexorable. She knows that some sort
of punishment, of disgrace, follows an act of this
kind. She fears, not for herself, but that her
much-loved husband —for whom the fault, if
fault it be, has been committed —will take
upon himself the responsibility, the shame,
the infamy, and she exhausts all the re-
sources of intrigue in her efforts to keep the fact
A G-REEDY MAN.
LITTLE BOY (passing with his father) loq.: “AIN’T THAT A GREEDY MAN, PAPA?
HE’S GOT ALL THE SEAT AND WON’T LET THE YOUNG LADY
HAVE ANY.”
a secret. Otherwise, her life is not very heroic-
She is petted, spoiled, played with, like any fool
of a doll of all the thousands who lead dolls’ lives
in all the civilised cities of our modern world. She
was taught by her father, from her cradle, that he
stood to her as God, conscience and propriety. She
is taught by her husband that a woman’s one
function is to be pretty, cheerful, and echoish of
her masculine master. She worships this husband
of her’s. He is only common, very common mud,
and she gilds him over with the gold of her
imagination. The climax comes ! The forgery is
no longer a secret between her and the mitn who
holds her father’s supposed bid. The husband is
informed, andthe'gold tumbles off the mud image,
and all her trust, confidence, and unwavering
devotion in and to this man fade into diaphaneity.
He scolds like a Billingsgate fish-fag, and displays
a littleness of soul degrading to a Bosjesman. Then
the holder of the bill relents. He will not make
the matter a public scandal. Thereupon the hus
band also relents, and informs disillusionised Nora
“that all is right as right can be.” She does not see
the absoluteness of the right and revolts. She feels
that her marriage has been a linked lie, a legalised
bodily degradation. She has lived with a
“ strange man,” she has begotten children bearing
his name, and the whole affair has no surer
foundation than the baseless fabric of a dream.
She leaves him, simply because she refuses to lead
a life which to her means degradation. The hus-
band of her fancy, her imagination, her
idealisation, is dead, and a pitiful, snivelling,
conventional puling cur, whose highest concern is
“ What will people think ?” stands before her in
all his realistic vulgarity—for the first time in her
life now truly realised —so small in his infinitesi-
malness of soul that she hardly sees him. So she
quits ! Who would wish her to stay after such a
climax? Who would desire between man and
woman any bond at all but that of an absolute
sincerity based upon a mutual knowledge, a
mutual sympathy, an identity of all those feelings
and passions which make a mai - riago something
more than a mere legal contract founded upon the
laws of property, tradition and primogeniture?
None surely but the “ Torvald Helmers ’ of the
cult that gathers with much show of superficial
ceremonial around the shrines sacred to the
goddess of a skin-deep faith—the Mrs. Grundy of
our modern, rotten, insincere and tottering
civilisation of shams, fakes, and artifices.
FRED J. BROOMFIELD.

What that ecclesiastical stock-exchange,
the Church of England, is coming to in England :
“What a splendid place these Roman Catholics have
got!” I remarked. “ Where is the Church of England
place?” “That he it, sir,” the man replied ; ' 1 that be
the parish church.” It was difficult to believe one’s
senses. The altar was a mass of light. Something like 50
or 60 candles of different heights were blazing away
amidst flowers and cunningly-arranged brasswork. At
the side, boys in surplices swung incense-dishes, and the
attendant priests bowed, twisted—aped the “genuflec-
tions of the many-pointed worm,” as Oliver Wendell
Holmes has it—and crossed themselves at intervals. The
nice little choir-boys in white and black came in proces-
sion, carrying a brass cross which was held aloft, while
others carried banners of various designs. Even the sexton
who opened the doors wore a long arrangement like a
black smock frock and had a girdle tied round his waist
like an ancient monk. When the congregation chn>°
out, the chief priest, vicar, rector, or whatever his
designation may he, came out with them. He was the
most laughable-looking figure I ever saw. A man cf
rather tall figure, he was dressed in black petticoats,
which came down to his feet; and the wind blew them
amongst his legs in comical fashion. Over this he wore a
'white surplice of half the usual length, and a sort of cape
over that. His face was clean shaven, and his hair trimmed
like a horse’s coat, while upon his head rested a black cap,
shaped like a three-cornered apple’ pastv. As he walked
through the churchyard, men doffed their hats, women
curtseyed, and children bowed low. and looked very
frightened. This is a fair specimen.of the usurpers who
have got possession of an English parish church—a church
kept by money extorted from the land and the people.
For six years the little kingdom of Greece
has been unable to procure an executioner from
among its citizens. During that time twelve
criminals have accumulated, and as nobody will
demolish them for any cash consideration, the
Government has at last agreed to pardon one of
them on condition that he kills the other eleven.
Most of the company refused even these terms,
but at last one —the meanest man in Greece —was
persuaded to accept the billet. The last Greek
execution took place in 1881, when some long-
standing arrears were worked off in the same way
—a person who had chopped his wife to fragments
behind the kitchen-door, and then said that some-
one else did it, being induced to officiate. The
Greek doesn’t rush after the job of obliterating
his fellow-man as the Englishman does, in fact,
things are so bad there that the officer now doing
temporary duty is being taken round under a
military escort, and even then lie has to be en-
close! in an iron cage to keep off the bricks that
are fired at him by everybody who sees him.
Also, the, soldiers have to hustle away a good
many people who try to prod him with red-hot
pokers through the bars of his residence, or to
poke him with'long sticks tipped with nails; and
when they are unable to get at him they call him
bad names instead, while he gnashes his teeth in
his hen-coop. A country which cannot raise a
stipendiary murderer has. left in it much that is
worthy of its immortal past.
The saying, “There is nothing new under
the sun,” is trite but true. In such a modern
“discovery” as Massage we might be excused if
we thought we bad some new thing, and yet a
reference to the library of Mr. Alfred Hamburg,
the eminent and well-known Masseur, of 112
Hunter-street, Sydney (close to Macquarie-sUyet),
proves that Massage was popular with the Chinese
3000 years ago; that the Laplanders and .Finns
employed it in pre-historic times; that the Japanese
looked upon it as a daily necessity ; ihat ancient
Germany held the treatment in the highest rever-
ences and that in the time of the Caesars the
Homans were “massirt ’daily by beautiful female
slaves, while all classes .used the treatment perhaps
less attractively, .but none the less effectively.
All reasonable members of the medical profession
now freely acknowledge the immense virtues of
“Massage,” and in-obstinate nervous, rheumatic,
and such like casts advise their patients to
have recourse to some reputable “Masseur.”
Mr. Hamburg- has been highly favoured in this
respect by all the leading medical men of Mel-
bourne, where he first rested after many years of
probation under the most prominent continental
Masseurs. The writer has seen scores of letters
and acknowledgments from these gentlemen and
from persons of the highest social rank in the
sister City, all couched in the most complimentary
language bearing witness to Mr. Hamburg’s fit-
ness for his work and the-success of his treatment.
The principle of Massage is entirely opposed to
our traditions of rest to every inflamed part, and
consists of a combination of procedures, which are
intended to rouse distorted and passive parts into
a natural condition of activity and health and
largely through the removal of abnormal deposits by
mechanical means. •
lii connection with the advertisement of
The New York Life Insurance Company appear-
ing on the front page of our present issue, we
desire to call attention to the prodigious growth
of this Company. For instance : The total amount
of • premiums received, 1880 to 1884, was
£9,177.163; and during the last five years, viz.,
1885 to 1889, £18,574,348. The amount of new
business last year written was £31,094,463, which
speaks for itself. Another conspicuous feature in
their statement is the interest received on invest-
ments, which during last year gave a daily
income of *2581. The income from interest and
rents has averaged 5J per cent, on its investments
for the past 10 years ; yet, so carefully are its
assets invested that only about one-half of one
per cent, interest on its investments remained un- -
paid Ist January, 1890, and a part of this was not
yet due. At that date the market value of its
bonds and stocks was £828,452 in excess of their
cost. The striking merit of the American In-
surance Law is the Tact that the State
is, so to speak, responsible for the sol-
vency of the Life Companies. The rriost
rigid accounts are exacted by the Government
department which has charge of life assurance
business ; and these accounts are closely scrutinised
by the officers of the department itself, all of
whom are trained and certificated actuaries. The
whole of the investments and securities are set out
with full detail in the Government issues; there-
fore the security of the New York Life is unques-
tionable. As new adaptations of insurance
principles have been worked out they have been
embodied, some in one form of policy, some in
another. But this company now embodies in a
single policy all the most notable and most valu-
able features which life insurance experts have yet
discovered. The improved methods introduced
and perfected by this office seem to be revolu-
tionising the business of life insurance here.

Business Announcements.
THREE GREAT LIGHTS!
The Needles Patent Gas-making Machine;
The Harvey Patent Safety Kerosene Lamp;
And the New Era Portable Gas Lamp.
The Harvey Lamp has been adopted by the
Railway Commissioners for lighting the Sydney
trams. We were awarded the Highest Order of
Merit and Special Mention by experts at the
late Melbourne Exhibition for our lighting
mediums. Over 6000 Harvey Lamps are in use
in this colony, and for all lighting purposes are
giving universal satisfaction. "These Lamps can
be seen on one of the North Shore ferry-boats,
and are suitable for all classes of vessels. The
Inventor offers £2OO to anyone producing as good
a Kerosene Burner.
